Preface
This thesis has been prepared over five months, from the 3rd of February to 3rd of July
2020, for the Department of Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark,
DTU, in partial fulfilment for the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng.
It is suggested that the reader has a basic knowledge in the areas of structural-, hygrother-
mal analysis and LCA.
The team and work methodology
The thesis team consists of two master students with different areas of expertise, different
methodologies were developed during the thesis period, to integrate the work between
each of the areas. One team member, s145492, has expertise within structural analysis
and thereby took charge of the structural aspects of the design. The other, s182746, has
expertise in energy and indoor climate, and took charge of the insulation and moisture
related properties of the element. Furthermore, the team has expertise in LCA, economy,
buildability and design from their studies. During the project some subjects were strictly
divided between the team members, but several were developed with knowledge from
both. How the thesis was divided between the parties can be found in A.13.
The concept of developing a prefabricated wall element was based on the desire to create
an alternative to some of the most polluting materials of the construction industry. The
analysed materials are hemp as insulation and bamboo as structure for the wall element.
This were chosen based on initial research about natural materials, as well as inspiration
from the companies HempCrete ApS and Holse&Wibroe at the Building Green Fair 2019.
This thesis was developed during the COVID-19 crisis in 2020, which began about a
month into the project. The crisis led to an adjustment of the evaluation and work methods.
The initial plan for the thesis was to develop a knowledge source based on tests and lab
based research. The element would be evaluated based on its tested performances and
compared to the ones of the competitors (prefabricated concrete/brick and wooden facade
element). Due to the restrictions, the team was only allowed a total of five days in the
concrete labs, where only the most relevant work was performed. This was determined
by figuring out which hypothesis were possible to prove or disprove with literature review
or theoretical based calculations; the remaining hypothesis were case based and needed
to be proven in the lab. This and the set design requirements were the determining factors
for the project development.
The communication and collaboration between the parties of the thesis, was also altered
due to the crisis. Which meant that all collaboration throughout March, April until the
middle of May was performed through video calls. Each week day, the two partners met
over online meeting on Microsoft Teams, where all day meetings were held. This way
discussions and issues could be resolved as they appeared, as well as it would be ensured
that the parties of the project were working in the same direction at all times.
Supervision meetings were as well held through Microsoft Teams once a week or second
week. Here the team had prepared drawings and questions for each session to gain the
most from them. No face-to-face supervision meeting was held after the 11th of March.

==PAGE:6==
Abstract
In this paper the design of a prefabricated wall element made of natural materials, hemp
and bamboo, is presented. The main goal is to create a solution that can compete with
conventional building techniques on a technical and economical level, while offering an
alternative which reduces the environmental impact of the building sector. Therefore, it
should be possible to implement the design proposal on a large scale in the main new con-
struction types in Denmark, such as five-storey buildings and single family houses. Hemp
and bamboo are chosen, based on their valuable properties, both are sustainable and
have favorable structural and hygrothermal characteristics. An iterative design process
is performed, where different design concepts are assessed based on their load-bearing
capacity, insulation- and moisture handling properties. The wall element is tested on a
theoretical level based on the Glaser method and with the software WUFI Pro, to deter-
mine the water content and moisture related issues. The strength of the bamboo elements
is calculated based on the theory for wood structures of the Teknisk Ståbi. The preferred
concepts are combined in possible wall designs and the elements’ buildability as well as
the production process are assessed, from which the best option is chosen. This element
design is evaluated based on its Life Cycle Assessment and costs, calculated with the
methods used in Sigma Enterprise. Finally, a prototype of the best option is built as a
scaled model, to evaluate all the practical challenges that could not be foreseen in the
previous theoretical design process. During the prototyping, the cohesiveness of the dif-
ferent materials is assessed as the combination of bamboo and hemp is state-of-the-art.
The end result is an optimized, integrated and simple prefabricated wall element, which
fulfils the set design requirements. Furthermore, it is cost wise a competitive solution and
has a lower environmental impact compared to the concrete/brick wall which is typically
used in Danish constructions. Further research and lab tests are required to make the
design proposal a market-ready product, but big potential is seen in the solution. The
implementation of this prefabricated wall alternative on a large scale, both for five-storey
building or in single-family houses, would reduce the overall environmental impact of the
construction phase in Denmark by 80% while still offering a high performing building en-
velope solution.

1
Introduction
1.1
Problem definition - Building sector nowadays
“
Energy efficiency and climate change: Buildings account for the largest
share of total EU final energy consumption (40%) and produce about 35%
of all greenhouse gas emissions [1].
”
The modern building sector has globally a major share of both the energy consumption
and the greenhouse gas emissions. This big impact on the environment has several rea-
sons, but it can roughly be divided in the following shares. The impact from fossil fuels
for energy and heating of buildings makes up more than 80% of these emissions. The
remaining share comes from the production phase of building materials, mainly steel, ce-
ramic, concrete or bricks and finally around 3% of the impact is from the end-of life phase
and disposal of building materials [2].
The building sector is not only contributing to climate change, but also to other impact
categories such as water and resource depletion, mainly from the material extraction. In
the EU, the sector is considered to be one of the main consumers of raw materials [3].
Where the commonly used material, cement, is the fourth most polluting one, after crude
oil, natural gas and oil. The pollution comes from the mining, high burning temperatures,
amounts of water and energy required for the production of cement [4]. After use, the
building materials end their lifespan as waste and landfill, where they may release dan-
gerous substances into the ground. Yet, there are very few recycling options.
All these factors prove the point that as it is now, the building sector needs to change
in a more sustainable and environmentally friendly direction. There are several attempts
to lower the impacts of the construction industry: regulations demand lower and lower
energy consumption for buildings, and there is big interest in the development of new
construction techniques with alternative building materials. However, to get the construc-
tion industry to change its traditional methods, the new alternatives, besides being the
environmentally preferred solution, must also be a technically and economically competi-
tive option to known building techniques. This means the alternative materials must have
similar functions, fulfill the same technical standards and be affordable cost- and time-
wise. Otherwise companies will have no interest in adopting the new ways and replace
the old ones on a large scale, and the environmental issue of the sector will not be tackled.
The challenge in this thesis is to address the issues mentioned above by developing an
alternative product with the natural materials hemp and bamboo. The reasoning for this
choice is elaborated in 1.3. This makes the main research question of the thesis: ”Is a
designed hemp-bamboo prefabricated wall element able to compete on a large scale with
the traditional materials used in today’s constructions?”.
1.2
The goal of this report
This report presents the development of an alternative solution to the traditional construc-
tion techniques. The challenge is to create an element that is both as far as possible CO2
neutral, and a design concept which can compete on the economical and technical levels,
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
1
==PAGE:12==
to make it appealing to companies and contractors. The idea is to develop a prefabricated
facade wall element made from the natural building materials hemp and bamboo. The el-
ement must be simple to manufacture and install on site, withstand strong climates and
provide a sufficiently supporting structure for a building of up to five storeys. Furthermore,
the element must perform well thermally and for what concerns moisture. The environ-
mental impact of the design is assessed by performing a Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) to
evaluate if there is any negative unforeseen impact. To assess the economy of the design,
a cost and time estimation is made to evaluate the business case for the element. Finally,
the prefabricated wall is compared to traditional solutions (prefabricated concrete/brick or
wood wall elements), to see in which situations the designed solution could replace the
old ones, and how the environmental impact of the building sector could be changed by
this.
To fulfill the requirements of the thesis, the design process to develop the wall element is
performed by following an innovative and creative iterative process. Where several dif-
ferent design concepts are evaluated on different levels (performance, aesthetics, build-
ability, and sustainability), and the combination of the best solutions converges in the final
prefabricated wall design. By using this process, based on the requirements set in 2.1,
the thesis approaches the best possible result for the designed solution.
1.3
Why hemp and bamboo?
There are a lot of alternatives when natural renewable building materials are considered.
In this report hemp and bamboo are chosen, because it is believed that besides hav-
ing very high sustainability potential as both plants grow fast, their structural and hygro-
thermal properties can potentially be combined into a highly competitive wall element.
1.3.1
Alternative materials for load-bearing structures - Bamboo
Figure 1.1: Bamboo ecosystem [5]
The most common materials for load-bearing structures are currently concrete, bricks and
steel, but also wood has been varying in popularity for construction throughout the ages.
As of now, wood construction is becoming more attractive, as the demands for lowering
the carbon footprint becomes higher. Conventional wood for construction in Denmark
includes the species: pine, spruce, oak and more. Complete wooden buildings often are
built with pinewood. The Danish forest holds more than 100 different wood species, and
2
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:13==
the world has even more potential wood species, but the species’ possibilities have yet
to be discovered. In the latest years different wood and plant species have entered the
building sector in Denmark, such as tropical wood and plants for flooring, cladding and
occasionally for visible beams and columns [6]. One of these species is bamboo, which
has become somewhat popular to use for items such as toothbrushes, clothes etc, with
the claim of being the sustainable substitute for other materials.
Looking deeper into the LCA of bamboo, there is a lot of truth in it, depending on the
context the material is used. Bamboo, which is a grass specie, has shown to be very
beneficial due to its fast growth and its wood-like properties. For now, bamboo is mostly
known in Denmark as a garden plant with very thin stems, but in other countries the plant
shows it self in a treelike appearance and it is used in construction in larger parts of Asia,
Africa and South America. In Denmark several companies are selling bamboo flooring
and cladding due to the strong resistance of the material. No company in Denmark has
yet copied the construction techniques of other parts of the world, despite the strength
potential of some bamboo species [7]. For this thesis, the MOSO bamboo specie is used
for all structural concepts, as this is the specie used by the bamboo flooring company
(Holse & Wibroe) who provided bamboo samples for the prototyping.
The reasoning for using bamboo instead of conventional wood, is that bamboo can be
planted in degraded or problematic land where wood plantation becomes hard or impos-
sible. Furthermore, bamboo can be harvested and utilized just 3 to 5 years after planting
the seeds. For conventional wood species, most takes more than 25 years from plant-
ing to harvesting for construction. The growing time for wood as well as the unforeseen
deforestation (fires, illegal logging etc.) demand a strict forest management to avoid too
large deforestation and still match the increasing needs of wood. Deforestation removes
vital spaces for animals and insects, as well as it decreases the potential release of oxy-
gen from the trees and the carbon storage [8]. In future, the deforestation issue could be
tackled, and balance between consumption and production could be established, but due
to the slow wood growth and the critical climate state of the earth, an immediate solution
is preferred. Therefore, cultivating and using bamboo can become a great alternative to
planting trees, especially if bamboo can perform as well as wood on most parameters and
even better on some.
Figure 1.2: MOSO Bamboo forest [9]
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
3
==PAGE:14==
The summed up arguments for planting bamboo for constructional purposes are the fol-
lowing:
• The rate of reforestation in China for bamboo is higher than for softwood in Europe.
This gives an opportunity for a faster reforestation of degraded land and less need
for forest management.
• The root to shoot ratio is higher for bamboo than for wood. This is due to the plant’s
extensive root system, which also stores most of the CO2 that the plant absorbs
from the atmosphere.
• Bamboo has the advantage of being a grass specie, meaning that it can be har-
vested without damaging the root system, which will keep shooting new bamboo
stems. This is unlike trees, that are usually clear cut, which can result in deforesta-
tion.
• Bamboo grows fast without requiring high maintenance, irrigation water or pesti-
cides.
• Some bamboo species have compression and flexural strength which is much higher
than for wood and is similar to concrete or steel, which makes the material highly
durable and efficient for bigger structures.
Information is gathered from [10].
1.3.2
Alternative materials for insulation - Hemp
The most common insulation materials used in the modern building sector are fiberglass,
rock wool, polystyrene or polyurethane foam. Even though these differ a lot between each
other there are some common characteristics between them. They have good insulating
properties and are known products, but they all have a high embodied energy for their
production. Either they are made out of plastic (fossil fuels) or require very high manufac-
turing temperature and only few of them are recyclable: they are not an environmentally
friendly choice ([11], [12]). Therefore, natural insulating materials should become more
and more popular. There are three different natural insulation types: animal-based such
as sheep wool, plant-based (hemp, straw, cotton or others) or mineral such as pearlite
and clay [13]. Every type of natural material has a lower environmental impact, because
it’s a renewable resource, it has less embodied energy, and sometimes it can even be
carbon neutral or passive when the embodied CO2 while growing (plant based solution)
is higher than what is required for its processing and transportation.
Hemp is the material which has been chosen for the development of the low impacting
wall element. This is due to the material’s special properties, as it is competitive option in
terms of thermal performance and moisture-related aspects (see next paragraph). This
makes the material, compared with the other alternatives, the best choice for a simple but
functional element design. The hemp will be assessed both in its fiber form, as insulation
panels, and in a hemp and lime mixture, which creates the so called hempcrete.
Hempcrete
Hempcrete is a composite material made from hemp shiv, the woody part of the plant
stem, and a lime based binder. The two materials are mixed with water to form a material
similar to concrete which is cast around a load bearing structure to make the composite
element. The structure is needed because hempcrete has low load bearing capacities,
and can only carry its self-weight. As any other composite material hempcrete has to cure
properly. It takes around 4 to 8 weeks [14], depending on the thickness of the element, to
4
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:15==
develop fully the mechanical properties and be dry, after which the finishing layer can be
applied on the element .
Figure 1.3: Hemp shiv, lime binder and water mixed together create the hempcrete com-
posite
The hemp-lime technique was developed in France in the eighties as a renovation tech-
nique. It was necessary to use a material that would keep the vapour permeability of
the wall around the old timber frame, and thereby maintain the building envelope in bal-
anced health between new and old elements [15]. The material also became popular in
other parts of Europe and in North America, not only as a renovation material but for new
constructions as well. This is because of the following qualities of the material:
• The good vapour permeability of hempcrete allows moisture to travel through the
wall. The permeability reduces moisture and mould issues, possible condensation
should dry out fast [16]. This leads to one of the main positive properties, as there
are fewer condensation issues, there is no need of extra layers such as a vapour
barrier, which allows a very simple and efficient construction [17].
• Hempcrete walls are good moisture buffers, which means that they can absorb and
release moisture fast enough to regulate and improve the indoor climate [18].
• Adding to the average insulation properties, thermal conductivity of 0.065 w/mK [19],
hempcrete also has a good thermal mass, since its density is between 220 and 950
kg/m3 [18]. This indicates the capacity to absorb and release heat, as it happens
slower than in other materials. The hot and cold peaks from outside are cut in the
indoor environment, improving the indoor climate quality.
• The high basic pH values of the lime binder should prevent the growth of pest inside
the element [17].
• It is a fire safe material, as it does not burn and there is almost no temperature
increase around the burning point [17].
• Hemp shiv is a renewable material as it is plant-based and Hempcrete is recyclable,
as it can be reused in new composites or decomposes in landfill without any harm
[15].
Furthermore, lime-based binders have less embodied carbon compared to other types
as for instance cement. This is because of the less energy demanding manufacturing
process and the fact that it re-absorbs carbon dioxide during the curing period, through
carbonization [20]. The hemp plant, on the other hand, grows with great adaptability in
different types of rich soil and climates, therefore it can be produced in several parts of
the word. It grows quite easily and fast, in four months the plant can reach a height of
1.5 to 4 m depending on the climate, without need of pesticides, herbicides or irrigation
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
5
==PAGE:16==
water. It can be planted in the rest year of the crop rotation resulting in a positive influence
on the soil. All parts of the plant (seeds, leaves, fibers, and so on) are used in different
applications, from the food and cosmetic sector, to the building and feed stock industry,
the shiv is the part that historically has the less value and use [15].
Hemp fiber panel
Apart from using the fibrous part of the hemp stem for paper and clothes, it is also possible
to create insulation panels out of the fibers. These are carded, pressed and heat treated
into the panel shape. This works because a smaller amount of plastic fiber is added,
which melts with the heat. The panels are a good solution for facade insulation, as they
have exceptionally good acoustic and thermal insulation properties (thermal conductiv-
ity of 0.039 w/mK [19]). Furthermore, it is a good substrate surface for plaster finishes
and is mechanically stable for horizontal loads. In general, the same moisture handling
properties apply to the hemp fiber as to the hemp shiv, therefore the combination of the
hempcrete and fibre insulation should maintain the same vapour-permeability as well as
”breathable” envelope characteristics presented in the previous paragraph ([21] and [22]).
Figure 1.4: Hemp panels (source: CalceCanapa®)
1.4
The construction sector in Denmark
Denmark and the Danish building sector are used as a reference in this thesis. The idea is
that a successful design could also be used in other similar contexts outside of Denmark.
The Danish building sector is presently highly active and the construction activity has been
at its highest in the last decade. It is enough just to look around to see that, especially
in urban context like Copenhagen or Aarhus, entire new neighbourhoods of multi-storey
buildings are been developed in the peri-urban areas. The demand of housing in the
city is constantly increasing and therefore, the urban growth has to happen very fast,
efficiently and at an affordable price [23]. The fast development only increases the use of
known material and construction technique, which is supported by long years of building
tradition and know how. The most common materials used in Danish building industry are
prefabricated reinforced concrete or light weight concrete elements, bricks and insulation
material such as EPS or rock wool, which are often put together in masonry cavity walls
(see figure 1.5) [24]. Furthermore, wood is becoming increasingly popular in Denmark
as a construction and cladding material, and several simple solutions with prefabricated
components are being developed, yet bricks and concrete remain the preferred option
[25].
6
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:17==
Figure 1.5: Typical wall layout for Danish modern construction
In figure 1.6 the new construction activity in Denmark of the last year can be seen (from
second quarter 2019 to first quarter 2020). This shows that the most build building types
are multi-storey buildings, single family houses and office buildings. The goal of this re-
port, is to develop and recommend a design for a prefabricated wall element which can
move the building sector in a more sustainable direction. Thus, the prefabricated wall
should be compatible for installation in multi-storey construction up to five storeys, in a
two storey row house design and single-family houses. If this can be achieved, the new
prefabricated wall concept could positively affect the environmental impact of the Danish
building sector.
Figure 1.6: Amount of new construction square meters build within the last year (from
2019 K2 to 2020 K1), subdivided per building type (source: Statistics Denmark)

==PAGE:18==

Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo

2
Methodology
2.1
Methodology and integrated design process
To design a competitive element and ensure the full use of its properties, several process
and design methodologies were used. Due to the Corona virus crisis, the team was only
allowed 5 days in the lab to create prototypes and perform tests of the highest relevance.
Therefore a combined theoretical and empirical approach to perform the element design
was developed for this thesis.
Before making use of the given days in the lab, most of the theoretical design methods
were put to use. Here the hemp and bamboo materials were assessed based on known
ways to use them. The different material concepts were evaluated with theoretical ap-
proaches: the structural analysis was based on the Eurocodes, uni- and bi-dimensional
calculation were made for U-values in Therm 7.7 and moisture simulations in WUFI Pro
for the hygrothermal analysis. The results from the assessments were combined in the
following design phase, here the integration of the different materials was of the highest
relevance. In this design phase the material concepts were put into different combina-
tions of elements, and the connections between them were assessed using 3D drawing
program SketchUp. The drawings, analysis and simulations raised several questions
throughout the process, which could not be answered by literature, as the materials are
new to the market. These questions were brought into the lab, where the creation of a pro-
totype of the prefabricated element was performed. The prototype was scaled down, as
a whole element would be to complicated to do by hand as well as move afterwards. The
prototyping was used as an empirical method designed to answer the questions which
were left unanswered by the theoretical approach.
Most issues were solved in an iterative process. If by choosing one solution for the el-
ement another issue would appear, the team would take a step back and find a simpler
solution which avoided too many issues or included quick fixes for them. The optimiza-
tion required integration between the different materials, compromises between the de-
sign subjects and constant communication between the team members. With this, the
process would preferably result in the most optimized product in terms of economy, sus-
tainability and technical performance.
2.2
Methodology and process for choosing the materials
Before defining the project description for the thesis, the main focus was chosen based
on research about sustainable materials. During this research several materials were
considered, but hemp and bamboo seemed to reappear, as they both are a sustainable
and renewable choice. They are already considered as building materials, but still in their
early stage of use and never in the combined version - there is a lot to be discovered.
The reason why the two materials were chosen, is given in Section 1.3. This thesis is
seen as an opportunity to explore the two materials and take them to the next step, the
construction industry.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
9
==PAGE:20==

Methodology and process for the prefabricated facade
element design
The design of the prefabricated facade element was based on of the following 6-step
research process.

Step 1: Determining requirements and appertaining evaluation

Several requirements were set based on building regulations, Eurocodes, sustainability
requirements etc. (see Table 2.1). A few requirements were determinants for the first
steps of the design process, these are highlighted in the figures below. For each further
step taken in the process, more requirements were considered. The requirements and
evaluation criteria for each design step were ruling out which designs and concepts would
be used in the further development.
Figure 2.2: Requirements leading to the choice of the materials hemp and bamboo
2.3.2

This step of the design process included research of different ways to use the materials
in buildings. Here a matrix of each possible material designs for both the structural and
10
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:21==
insulation materials was developed. The matrix (see Appendix A.1) was used to get an
overview of the possible material’s use concepts and the inspiration of possible combina-

reasoning and theory which is elaborated in Chapter 3.
2.3.3
Step 3: Evaluation of material designs
When developing further the design concepts, parameters such as low U-value and high
load bearing capacity were considered in the evaluation of each material design. In this
phase some of the material designs were discarded as they did not meet the evaluation
criteria.
Figure 2.3: Requirements for step 3: Evaluation of material design
2.3.4

The final wall element was designed in step 4, based on the resulting best options of the
material designs from step 3. For this step two additional parameters were added in order
to discard further material designs. The buildability and the prototyping were assessed
to analyze the combination further. This step determined the final prefabricated element
design which was evaluated in step 5.
Figure 2.4: Requirements for the wall element design
2.3.5
Step 5: Evaluation of the prefabricated element
When evaluating the final designed prefabricated wall element, additional requirements
and appertaining evaluation criteria were considered in order to optimize the solution.
The requirements and evaluation criteria are all notated in Table 2.1. Whether the final
element meets the criteria is stated in Chapter 4.

potential (emitted kg of CO2 eq). With this, it is possible to assess the environmental
impact (emitted kg of CO2 eq) of implementing the design in the construction section, as
well as the economical viability of the solution.

2.4.2
Structural analysis
Each of the structural concepts is analysed based on load of a one storey building, with
a flat roof and concrete slabs. Each of the concepts is evaluated based on its strength
capacity.
Loads
The capacity calculations are based on prerequisites from Teknisk Ståbi [26], the wood
structure analysis is taken from [27], [28] and [29], loads are determined using the follow-
ing Eurocodes:
• DS/EN 1990 Basis for project engineering for load carrying structures DS/EN 1990
DK NA
• DS/EN 1991-1-1 Densities, self-weight and live loads for buildings DS/EN 1991-1-1
DK NA
• DS/EN 1991-1-2 Fireloads DS/EN 1991-1-2 DK NA
• DS/EN 1991-1-3 Snowloads DS/EN 1991-1-3 DK NA
• DS/EN 1991-1-4 Windload DS/EN 1991-1-4 DK NA
• DS/EN 1991-1-5 Thermal loads DS/EN 1991-1-5 DK NA
• DS/EN 1991-1-6 Loads on constructions during excecution DS/EN 1991-1-6 DK NA
The specific loads and self weights of the materials are taken from:
Imposed loads
DS/EN 1991-1-1:2007
Self weight concrete
DS/EN 1991-1-1:2007
Self weight hempcrete
CalceCanapa®
Self weight hemp fibers
CalceCanapa®
Self weight laminated bamboo
[30]
Self weight raw bamboo
[30]
14
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:25==
All loads are considered to be carried by the bamboo structure in each element, which
distributes the loads between elements and to the foundations. All loads, including im-
posed, live, natural loads and self-weight are considered in load combinations. Concrete
slabs are used for the design, as concrete slabs are commonly used and would create the
largest load on the structure. This ensures that the element can be used in all situations,
both for new buildings, and as a substitute element in renovation. All bamboo elements
are calculated as if they were timber structures, as no standards for bamboo structures
are available and certified yet.
Load bearing capacity
Firstly the different structural concepts are compared by load bearing capacity. The pre-
ferred option for the element design, is when a high load bearing capacity is reached
with the lowest amount of bamboo material in the structure. An example of the calcula-
tion method can be found in Appendix A.3. The final structural concept is chosen based
on the findings and the other set requirements, afterwards the elements are tested and
adjusted to a capacity matching a five storey building.
Capacity of beams
The upper beam in the structure obtains the load from the slabs and distributes it to the
supporting columns. The beams are designed for both roof slab and indoor slab, as the
load combinations for each scenario give different load sizes. The beams are designed
as simply supported beams, spanning between two columns with 0.6 meters, as this is
the worst case scenario. The dimensions for the beams in the element are determined
by the necessary moment of resistance Wy, which is designed by the maximum moment
MEd created by the load, p, on the beam. Furthermore, the beams are validated against
cracks due to shear, lateral buckling and deflection < L/400.
Properties for elements
Length of beam:
L [m]
Area:
A = w × h [m2]
Moment of resistance:
Wy = 1/6 × w × h2 [m3]
Moment of inertia:
Iy = 1/12 × w × h3 [m4]
Point load:
P [kN]
Distributed load:
p [kN/m]
Max moment:
MEd = 1/8 × p × L2 [kNm]
Bending capacity of beam

fmd [MPa]
Bending stress:
σm = MEd/Wy
Utilization of flexural strength:
U = σm/fmd ≤1

kcr = min(3/fvk or 1)

wef = kcr × w

Capacity of columns
For the columns in each solution, the stresses in the cross section are calculated in order
to determine the necessary moment of resistance. A cross section for the column is set
and the column reduction factor is determined due to the slenderness, then the stresses
in the column can be calculated. Finally the utilization of the columns capacity, based
on the stresses from the vertical and horizontal loads, are examined. All columns are
calculated as simply supported columns, and slenderness of the columns is taken into
account. Furthermore, the columns are validated against cracks due to shear, lateral
buckling and deflection < L/400.
Capacity of columns

√

Ls = 1 × L

The shear capacity and deflection of the columns are designed with the same method as
for the beams, using the horizontal loads on the column.
The capacities of the beams and columns are based on the strength given by the scientific
report for laminated and raw bamboo [30]. No tests are performed to verify the material
strength.
2.4.3
U-value calculation
The Danish building regulation gives strict requirements on the energy use of a building.
The stated energy frames to be fulfilled, apply to several technical aspects of the con-
16
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:27==
struction such as heating, cooling, DWH and ventilation. The performances of the build-
ing envelope surely influence the energy consumption through heat losses, infiltration,
thermal bridges and more. The BR18 [31] states the following requirements concerning
the building envelope excluding windows and doors.
• For new buildings the U-value of external walls can maximum be 0.30 W/m2K (Annex
2: Tables for Chapter 11, Table 1)
• In the voluntary Building Class 2020 for the low energy classification, the following
stricter requirements for design transmission losses trough the envelope are stated
(§ 476):
Voluntary low energy class [W/m]
Single-storey
3.7
Two-storey
4.7
Three or more storey
5.7
Table 2.3: Summary of low energy class targets in BR18
Figure 2.6: Recommended U-values to fulfill the Danish energy framework (source: Rock-
wool).
The U-value for the elements is calculated, both in a uni-dimensional (see formula) and
in a bi-dimensional way with the software Therm 7.7; this to consider also the bamboo
structure inside the element and see if it creates thermal bridges and affects the thermal
performance of the wall.
To calculate the U-value for a wall the following formula is used:
U =
1
(Rso + R1 + R2 + R3 + Rn + Rsi)[W/m2K]
Where R is the resistivity of each wall layer calculated as R = l/λ[m2K/W] (λ is the
thermal conductivity and l the thickness of the layer), 1 to n indicates the layers of the
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
17
==PAGE:28==
wall, Rsi and Rso indicate the fixed internal and external resistance. The lower the U-
value is, the better are the insulating performances of the considered wall element. The
aim in this research is to see which of the considered options has the lowest U-value.
2.4.4
Moisture evaluation
The Danish building regulation has a specific chapter related to moisture (Chapter 14 in
BR18 [31]), in this no specific limits, but overall requirements are set. Water, moisture
contents and condensation should not damage the building components or create health
risks. There should not be issues with mould and fungus growth, especially related to
thermal bridges, and no penetration of water from the ground, from rain or melt-water
should happen [31].
To fulfill these requirements, moisture evaluation and simulations are made, both in a
stationary and in a non stationary way. For the stationary ones, the Glaser method is
used, which calculates and assesses the moisture transport in a building component by
considering the vapour diffusion throughout its layers. The amount of possible interstitial
condensation for the winter months can be calculated, and it can be assessed whether
the wall dries out during the summer months. If this happens and if generally the conden-
sation amounts are low, then the building component is considered safe [32].
The needed input data for the calculations for each material in the assembly is the thermal
conductivity λ[mK/W] and the vapour permeability δ[kg/msPa]. Generally building ma-
terial companies provide the water vapor diffusion resistance factor µ[−] for their products,
this is the proportionality coefficient between the vapor permeability of air and the one of
the considered material. Therefore to obtain the parameter needed for the calculations
the following inverted formula was used:
δ = δ0
µ [kg/msPa]
Where δ0 is the the vapor permeability of an ideal air layer, 2×10−10 kg/msPa [33]. The
complete calculation process for the Glaser method can be found in Appendix A.5.
The Glaser method has some limits, especially if very vapour permeable materials, such
as the one chosen for this report (see Chapter 3.1), are considered. It only evaluates
moisture transport through vapor diffusion and not the capillary moisture transport or the
sorption capacity, which reduces the risk of damage due to condensation in permeable
materials [34]. All material parameters are constant and excess water appears as con-
densation, which is not true for hygroscopic materials. Anyhow, as it is a fast and simple
tool and it is considered to be on the safe side due to its strict boundary conditions, it was
chosen for a pre-assessment of the hygrothermal behaviour of the design concepts.
To overcome the limits of the Glaser method, the permeable properties of hemp need
to be included and a more realistic simulation of moisture conditions can be obtained.
To perform the simulations the software WUFI® Pro was used. The software calculates
one-dimensional non-stationary hygrothermal profiles for a cross-section [35], consider-
ing also events such as construction moisture, driving rain and solar radiation.
The required input information consists of the layer layout of the component, its orien-
tation, surface transfer coefficient and initial conditions. The simulation time period, the
outdoor climate and building location as well as the indoor climate conditions are required.
Furthermore, six main parameters must be inserted for every considered building mate-
rial: the bulk density [kg/m3], the material porosity [m3/m3], the specific heat capacity
[J/kgK], the thermal conductivity [W/mK], the water vapor diffusion resistance factor
18
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:29==
[−] and the typical built-in moisture [kg/m3]. Finally for each material the moisture stor-
age function needs to be inserted in the program.
The simulations are performed with an iterative process, where only one parameter is
changed at a time, to assess how much this influences the results (see Section 3.3.3).
Overall, the following parameters are evaluated to interpret the simulations:
• Total water content of the wall, this must be low and become stationary over the
years, otherwise it means that the wall is soaking up water over time.
• Water content in each layer, if this oversteps certain limits it could deteriorate the
material and affect its properties. No specific recommended water content levels
were found for the assessed materials, but to prevent rot in organic materials (wood
or natural fibers) the water content should not exceed 20 mass-percent over long
periods of time. This considers a safety margin as wood rot fungi starts the de-
composing process at a moisture content of 25-30% [36]. The limit is taken as a
guideline: moisture content exceeding this levels for short time can be tolerated if
temperatures are low (no fungi grows with low temperatures); furthermore the sorp-
tion capacity and high PH value of hempcrete should prevent moisture issues [16]
and [17].
• The relative humidity (RH) in certain monitoring points in the wall. In permeable ma-
terials there is a gradual transition between sorption moisture and dew water, there-
fore it is not possible to identify a specific amount of condensation water. However
if RH is close to 100% free saturation condition is reached, which means a large
amount of water is transported to the considered point. This could on the long term
have negative effects on the material.
• Effects of driving rain.
• Possible mould growth on indoor surface, by looking at the temperature-moisture
diagrams and the limiting curves LIM B I and LIM B II [36].
• Loss of thermal insulation capacity (thermal conductivity increasing with moisture
contents).
• Codependency of moisture levels in between the adjacent materials.
2.4.5
Buildability
To determine whether the element was possible to be manufactured and installed on site,
3D drawings, detail drawings and prototyping are performed.
3D drawings of wall element
SketchUp was used to reveal whether a combination of the two materials leads to issues
in the system integration, as well as determining whether the system could become a
simple system with a minimum of steps to be performed on the building site.
Detail drawings of the wall element
Detailed drawings were performed in order to determine the connections between the
elements and with other building components, as well as to design how openings for
windows and doors can be implemented.
Prototyping in concrete lab
Prototyping was performed, in order to prove or disprove the hypothesis made throughout
the research and the development of the prefabricated wall element. The team was given
five days to work in the concrete lab in 119 at DTU, where three different prototypes were
built.

2.4.6
Sustainability
The sustainability of the design is assessed by performing a cradle-to-gate Life Cycle As-
sessment (LCA) of the final wall element. The resulting global warming potential (emitted
kg of CO2eq) is used to evaluate the actual impact of the design, analyse whether there
are some hot spots in the production phase and compare environmentally the solution to
the traditional building techniques (prefabricated concrete/brick or wood wall).
To perform the LCA, the standard ISO 14044 [37] is taken as reference. The standard
states requirements and guidelines which were followed when performing the LCA. The
different steps of an LCA are:
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
21
==PAGE:32==
• Goal and scope definition: The goal and scope of the LCA must be defined before
starting. This includes defining the reason for performing the LCA, what the calcu-
lation is used for, the object of it, the method and who is going to use the results.
• Life cycle inventory analysis: The life cycle of the considered calculation object
is researched in detail, this consists of data collection for all the input materials and
processes which the considered object goes through.
• Life cycle impact assessment: The third step in an LCA consists of the actual
impact calculation. In this case, the calculation was performed with the software
SimaPro by using the input data from the previous step for the hemp-bamboo wall.
The walls used in the comparison were assessed in program LCAbyg by using sam-
ples found in the software’s database.
• Interpretation phase: The results are assessed whether they are feasible and their
significance is interpreted. In this step the hot spot analysis is performed, and if
mistakes are found, corrections of the model are performed.
• Completeness and consistency check: It must be assessed whether the input
data set is complete or if major processes were left out, and if the the assumptions
are consistent through out the model.
• Sensitivity analysis: The sensitivity analysis is performed to measure the relia-
bility of the results. The calculations are performed again by changing some of
the inserted quantities or processes to see how much these influence the result.
Furthermore, the calculation method can also be changed to assess if the overall
resulting impacts are correct and reliable.
• Reporting and critical review: The results are presented in a report, and in some
cases they must go through a critical review by an external expert.
A LCA is performed on an object which is called the functional unit of the LCA. In this
case the final wall element from the design process is taken as functional unit. When
a comparative LCA is performed, where different products with the same function are
compared based on their impact, it is important that the same functional unit is used for
them all. This is why, when the results of the hemp-bamboo wall LCA were compared to
the one of the other wall elements, it was important that the same dimension in width and
height as well as the same insulating properties (U-value) and load-bearing capacities
were considered.
The used calculation method is the CML-IA baseline. The choice derives from the fact
that, in general, building companies as well as the DGNB system use this calculation
system for their certification [38]. Furthermore, it is the methodology used by the LCAbyg
software, which was used for the LCA of the two reference wall elements. The considered
impact category for the comparative LCA is climate change, which is expressed with the
global warming potential for time span of 100 years (GWP100). The calculated results
give the potential greenhouse gases emitted in the air, as emitted kg of CO2 equivalent
[39]. The LCA is performed in a cradle-to-gate version, which means that the wall element
is considered until the moment it is installed on site and is ready for use. This is because
only a wall element and not an entire building design is considered, therefore there is no
available information for the use phase. Furthermore, the three different assessed wall
types have the same thermal properties, and thereby, the same amount of energy goes
into the building during the use phase, and their impacts should be identical.
Maintenance and end-of-life stage are discussed outside of the LCA calculation as, again,
22
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:33==
there is not enough information about the materials’ disposal, which means that too many
assumptions would be made.
The sensitivity is performed by assessing the ILCD 2011 Midpoint+ calculation methodol-
ogy for the overall result evaluation. And the most impacting processes were increased/de-
creased by 10% in their quantity or changed with alternative similar processes, to asses
their weight on the final impact. By doing this, hot spots and future focus points for the
product development were possible to detect.
The results of the LCA for the prefabricated wall-element can be found in Chapter 4.5.2
while the comparison with the other building techniques can be found in Chapter 5.2.2.
2.5
Programs
For the development of the wall element several programs were used to prove and support
the hypothesis made for the solutions.
Excel was used in order to develop a program for the structural concept. This ensured that
any change or optimization of the structural elements was simplified. To support the re-
sults from the Excel program several hand calculations were performed for the elements.
Furthermore, the software was used for the uni-dimensional U-value calculations, the
Glaser method, and the Life Cycle Inventory for the LCA.
WUFI Pro was used to perform non-stationary hygrothermal simulation on the assessed
wall cross-sections. The results from the simulations were interpreted to see whether the
wall section would perform well or have moisture related issues.
Therm 7.7 was chosen to perform bi-dimensional U-value calculation of the assessed
wall layouts. The program also shows the isotherm lines in the section, and if there would
be any risk of thermal bridges in the building component.
Sketch up was utilized for all 3D drawings, as the program creates a simple frame for
the 3D work. This ensured the team a way to assess the theoretical integration of the
materials as well as buildability.
Autocad was used for all the detailed 2D drawings of the element.
Fire con was used to calculate the minimum thickness of the materials which insulates
the combustible structure from a fire. Whether or not the combustible structure would
be sufficiently insulated from a design fire for at least 60 minutes, is determined by the
temperature the structure is exposed to, within that time frame. The temperature limit
for the combustible material was set to 200 degrees C°. The results determined the wall
element’s fire resistance on a theoretical level.
Simapro was used to perform the LCA of the final wall element. In the program’s database
it is possible to find most common manufacturing processes and materials, but it is also
possible to create new ones. This was necessary for some of the assessed processes as
hemp and bamboo are not common materials.
LCAbyg was utilized for the LCA calculations of the competitive wall elements so that the
comparison with the hemp-bamboo elements could be made. The program was chosen
as its database contains all the processes and materials for the most common building
techniques. The calculation method from this program was used as well in Simapro, to
ensure the same method for all elements, so that the comparison between them would
be possible.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
23
==PAGE:34==
Sigma estimate was used in order to understand the costs of the element and to compare
it with the competitors. Information about the competitive elements was collected from
the Molio database and a process was developed for the hemp-bamboo element based
on prices given by the reference companies and man-hours for similar processes. The
results from the program were assessed considering that some input data, as for instance
man-hours, are uncertain because of the process novelty.
24
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:35==
3
Research
3.1
Introduction to the element design
All evaluations and designs were based on creating system integration between the dif-
ferent subjects and the different materials in the final element. The integration was mainly
performed in the buildability assessment of the final element, but it is also represented in
many of the set requirements, which are dependent on each other (see Chapter 4).
3.1.1
Structural concepts
Different alternatives for the load bearing structure made out of bamboo were found based
on research. Three different concepts were found interesting to investigate further, and
are analysed in the evaluation of the structural concept 3.2.
Figure 3.1: From the left: structural concept 1: Bamboo stem structure, concept 2: lami-
nated bamboo structure and concept 3: ”LEGO”-block structure
Structural concept 1: Bamboo stem structure
The bamboo stem structure was inspired by the most traditional way of using bamboo, and
different techniques for using and connecting them have already been developed. The
bamboo stems can be joined in a low tech way, by tying the stems together or in a high
tech way, where steel joints are designed to distribute the loads from one stem to another.
These methods can be used either with single stems or a combination of more stems to
create beams and columns, if more strength is needed. This concept was appealing to
use, due to the few steps to produce a bamboo stem for constructional purpose, which
makes the concept seem very sustainable. The concerns for this concept were mostly the
possibilities of creating a prefabricated element with a standardized solutions to connect
elements.
Structural concept 2: laminated bamboo structure
The laminated bamboo structure is the solution closest to the building style used today,
when it comes to wood structures. To create laminated bamboo, bamboo strips are cut,
glued and pressed together, a process similar to glue laminated timber. In this project,
laminated beams and columns are joined to create a bamboo version of a stud wall. This
concept was appealing due to the strength certainty which the lamination method creates,
ensuring that flaws in the bamboo do not weaken the strength of beams or columns. So by
gluing pieces of bamboo together it lowers the impact of imperfections [40]. The concerns
for this concept were mainly the impact of the added glue and the long process of creating
a beam or column in glue laminated bamboo.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
25
==PAGE:36==
Structural concept 3: ”LEGO”-block structure
For the ”LEGO”-blocks columns or stems of bamboo are cast in a block of hempcrete, with
a part sticking out of the block to fit in the block above. This has to fit with the columns in the
block above, from where the ”LEGO”-name comes. The idea was particularly interesting
if proved that the one size block could fit most possible projects. The main concerns for
this concept were whether it had the capacity for a multistorey building.
3.1.2
insulation concepts
The following presented options and their combination are assessed in this report as insu-
lation and infill alternatives for the prefabricated facade element design (see Section 3.3).
The hemp-lime mixture can be used in three different ways, furthermore also a hemp fiber
possibility is assessed.
Figure 3.2: From the left: insulation concept 1: hempcrete cast around structure, concept
2: hempcrete pre-cast into blocks or panels, concept 3: hempcrete mixture sprayed in
situ
Insulation concept 1: hempcrete cast around structure
This is the typical way of using hempcrete, the composite mixture is prepared and then
poured and tapped in apposite form-works around the load-bearing structure. This pro-
cedure is normally made by hand on site. In this report it needs to be assessed whether
it is possible to cast prefabricated elements with a load-bearing structure in the factory
and then transport them on site, to optimize and possibly automatize the casting and
installation method.
Insulation concept 2: hempcrete blocks or panels
The hempcrete blocks or panels are created in a factory, with or without inserted load
bearing structure and can be of various dimensions. They are used as a regular brick and
jointed with mortar on site, although they must be combined with a load bearing structure.
The positive aspect is that the elements can be cast, cured and stored before an order is
received. No specific design is needed for a project, but they can be mass-produced for
any application.
Insulation concept 3: Sprayed hempcrete on site
In this case the hempcrete is sprayed by machine in a specific one side open form-work
around the load bearing structure on site. It is considered the most efficient and fast way
of casting, and makes it easier to cast around structures and other delicate places. The
method, although, is quite expensive and requires specific machinery, furthermore it is
used only for construction in-situ. It will be assessed whether the machines can be used
in a factory to optimize the construction of the prefabricated elements, or if they can be
used for finishing work on site.
Insulation concept 4: hemp fiber panel
Adding to the hempcrete options, also hemp fibers can be used as insulation in external
facade walls. These come in fiber panels and have a similar layout and application as
26
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:37==
wood fibers panels or Rockwool insulation. This infill option needs to be combined with
other element components, as it needs support to carry the horizontal loads.
3.2
Evaluation of structural concepts
3.2.1
High load bearing capacity
The properties for both hempcrete and bamboo were taken either from the reference com-
pany or from scientific reports which would assess the same type of material as the ones
of this report. This was done to get the most coherent results possible. For the hemp
materials all properties were found based one the given values from the company Cal-
ceCanapa® [19], as the company has given all the properties needed for the materials.
From here, the compressive strength of hempcrete was found to be between 0.4 and 2.5
MPa. Though as the Youngs modulus was not given by the company, it was determined
from [41] by choosing the mixture with the same strength as the hempcrete mix used in
the design. For the bamboo structure, all used properties are based on findings from
[30]. This report was used as it tests laminated MOSO bamboo, which is the same bam-
boo specie used in this report. Furthermore, the tests presented in [30] gave the results
needed to perform the essential analysis of the elements.
The high load-bearing capacity assessment is a part of step 3, ”Evaluation of material
designs”, of the design process. All three mentioned structural concepts were analysed
as a possibility for the final structure. The formulas used to calculate the capacities were
based on [27], [28] [29], [26] and are presented in 2.4.2.
In the following tables the material properties for both raw and laminated bamboo, as well
as for the hempcrete are presented:

Comparison of structural concepts
The load-bearing capacity and stability of each of the three solutions were compared
with the amount of material used to produce one element. The material amount was
determined based on one element carrying the load from one storey including self weight,
which simplified the selection process. All solutions were designed to carry the vertical
and horizontal loads on the bamboo structure without taking the hempcrete into account.
All calculations are based on the calculation methods for timber, as no regulations have
been issued and certified for bamboo in Denmark.

Bamboo stem structure
Figure 3.3: Bamboo stem structure
The load bearing capacity of the bamboo structure was found to be very interesting due to
the geometry and strength of raw bamboo. Furthermore, when looking at the material use
for a wall element, the bamboo stem structure needed the least amount of material of the
three concepts to gain the needed capacity to carry the load of a one storey building. The
stems also showed great potential as they are ready to use only after a treatment and a
drying period of 2-3 months. On the other hand the geometry and composition of the fibers
in a bamboo stem, can create issues in terms of standardizing a prefabricated element
with them, especially if several stems have to be combined to obtain the load bearing
capacity. The geometry of the stems can make the casting of hempcrete around them
28
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:39==
difficult, and this could create thermal bridges. Furthermore, the fibers in the stem are all
in the longitudinal direction, which makes its splitting very likely, especially when using
nails and screws trough the material. Therefore, very low tech connections are normally
created with ropes, or high tech connections of steel are built for specific applications.
LCA wise, the stems make a good material due to the few process steps and non toxic
treatments. But as the geometry of the stems makes up a large volume, additional space
is needed for the same amount of material, compared to bamboo strips. This makes the
transport costs in terms of CO2 quite high compared to the remaining phases for the stem
[42]. Even though the solution showed great promise, the limitations of the connections
between elements and integration with the insulation made a simple manufacturing and
installation process out of question.
Laminated bamboo structure
Figure 3.4: Laminated bamboo structure
The glue laminated bamboo structure demands approximately the same amount of mate-
rial as for the bamboo stems to gain the needed strength capacity. The laminated bamboo
structure has similar strength capacities as the bamboo stems, but is also influenced by
the glue in the material. The whole process of creating a glue laminated bamboo element
is long and costly, but it ensures that the strength is uniform throughout the element. The
bamboo stems can have different strength capacity in different parts of it, for instance the
notes are stronger than the internodes. Furthermore, the concept is very similar to the
known techniques of glue laminated timber which makes the transition from the traditional
work method easier. This solution does not have limitations in terms of creating connec-
tions with screws and bolts and the CO2 emission from the transport of the elements is
lower, as the strips takes up a smaller volume on a transport. Due to this solution’s triv-
ial limitations, compared to the other two, the team chose to proceed with this concept.
Therefore the rest of the requirements will only be developed for this structural concept.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
29
==PAGE:40==
”LEGO”-block structure
Figure 3.5: ”LEGO”-block structure
The blocks are cast in hempcrete with poles of laminated bamboo inside, where the
hempcrete creates the insulating element and the bamboo the structural concept for the
solution. The smart thing about these blocks, is that they can be produced and cured
before an order is received. The blocks would not be designed for a specific project, but
mass-produced for any project. When analysing the compressive strength capacity of the
elements, different scenarios were developed in order to create the highest capacity with
the lowest material use.
Hemp Blocks
Figure 3.6: Development of hemp blocks
The initial amount of poles was inspired by an actual manufacturing company, which cre-
ates blocks of hempcrete with structural poles inside. Here eight poles are cast in the
hempcrete, and the blocks are already created with plaster. This method was tested with
bamboo poles, which resulted in slender poles with a too low capacity for the horizontal
loads on the facade. From this, the hemp blocks were further developed with an Excel
program created for the purpose by the team. The different options were tested, the first
reasonable solution for the hemp blocks, was to create them with four poles instead of
eight. The poles were still placed in two rows. For the compression from slabs and self
weight, this was the best solution, only the horizontal load from wind still created issues.
The bamboo itself did not have the capacity to withstand the wind load, due to its slen-
derness. Though due to the hempcrete around the bamboo, the block was theoretically
able to withstand the wind load. Anyhow, due to the fact that the bamboo and hempcrete
can possibly detach and not act as a coherent material, this is not a reliable result. If the
bamboo poles alone should be able to withstand the horizontal loads, the poles should be
of such size, that the solution would become somewhat the same as the laminated bam-
boo structure 3.2.1. This issue is added to the eccentricity which likely would be created
due to non-precise production and placement of the blocks. The bamboo poles must be
produced precisely and placed exactly on top of each other to avoid this issue. As this is
highly unlikely, the solution was discarded for the final element.
30
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:41==
3.2.2
Stable construction
The concept’s capacity was examined through the horizontal loads based on the Eu-
rocodes for wind actions [43].
Figure 3.7: Element with bracing system
To stabilize the laminated bamboo structure against wind load, a bracing system was im-
plemented. The bracing system design was based on the windload on the perpendicular
wall that the element must withstand. The design load for each truss was Ft = 17, 97kN,
see Validation of trusses Appendix A.2. One truss per direction in each facade side must
be placed per floor. To ensure this, one element is designed to contain the two braces,
so only one element per facade side must contain trusses. The trusses were designed
in stainless steel to avoid any corrosion in the element. Besides stainless steel trusses,
also bamboo trusses were validated in terms of dimensions, as bamboo has a large tensile
strength. See Table 3.4 for the material use and capacity for each material.
Material

Table 3.4: Details for wind trusses in steel and bamboo
The laminated bamboo is considered to be less impacting in terms of CO2, and would be
preferred in a sustainable view. But due to the simplicity and low costs of the prefabricated
solution of the steel wind trusses, this solution was preferred for the final element.

3.3.1
U-value calculation
Four different layer structures for the wall were considered for the U-value calculation. In
the first the bamboo structure is cast in hempcrete, as it would be in the traditional in-situ
construction. The second one has a mixed wall construction with outer Hempcrete panels,
supported by the bamboo structure which is filled in with hemp fibers insulation. The third
is a hempcrete block wall construction. The last one is similar to the first, but with a thinner
hempcrete layer and an additional outer insulation layer. All cases are plastered on the
internal and external side with a lime based vapour permeable plaster. A specific lime
plaster mixed with hemp shiv was considered, as other alternative, for the external side of
the wall to see how much it would improve the hygrothermal performance of the wall. In
Table 3.5 a summary of the technical data of the considered materials can be found. For
all the U-value calculation it was taken into account that laminated bamboo beams were

In this layout, the RH never exceeds 100% even though between the external plaster and
the hemp fiber panel, in January it gets to 99.6% and in February to 97.3%.
Generally, the weakest spot is between the external plaster and the hempcrete or hemp
fiber layer, because of the different vapour permeability of the two adjacent materials.
But with the different plaster (the thermoplaster or the finishing for Wall 4) there were no
issues, as these have higher vapour permeability. It is therefore very important to chose
the right plaster as finishing. The calculated amount of condensation in Wall 1 and 2 are
very small, and are acceptable.
To conclude, the results were quite worrying for Wall layout 3, as the entire external
hempcrete panel exceeds the 100%, this means that the entire layer is wet and soaked
with water, this could affect the material properties and make it deterioration faster. Fur-
thermore, the high humidity levels affect also the hemp fiber layer and the load-bearing
structure in between the hempcrete panels. As the Glaser method has the limits pre-
sented in Chapter 2.4.4, it is not possible to evaluate further, the simulations in WUFI are
necessary to assess the issue.
3.3.3
Moisture evaluation - WUFI Pro simulations
Several non-stationary simulations were performed in WUFI Pro, but mainly wall layout
2.2 and wall layout 4 were assessed, as they were the preferred U-value options. As said
in Chapter 2.4.4, these were evaluated with an iterating process where parameters would
be change one at a time. The variable parameters were:
• Orientation (south or north)
• Different types of external plaster (critical point in Glaser method)
• The addition of a possible rain screen
• The geographical location, either Scandinavian climate (Lund, Sweden) or Mediter-
ranean climate (Marseille, France)
• Build-in moisture from the composites at the start of the simulation period
The main input data, material properties and their sources, as well as climate files and
other data taken from the WUFI Pro database can be found in Appendix A.5. Each as-
sessed wall type got optimized by changing the parameters iteratively and keeping the
best results. In the following paragraph the main evaluations will be summarized. The
complete graphs and comments can be found in Appendix A.5.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
37
==PAGE:48==
South vs. north facing walls located in Lund:
In the simulations there is a big difference between south and north facing facades, and
surprisingly the second one is performing better. This is because driving rain in Lund
mainly come from the south (see Figure 3.14) and the main source of water in the wall
elements is absorbed rain, which does not dry out fast enough. So even though the north
facing wall has less exposure to sun, it has a lower moisture content.
Figure 3.14: From left, solar radiation and driving rain diagram for Lund, Sweden
Different types of external plaster:
As seen with the Glaser method calculation, the features of the external plaster can in-
fluence the water content of the wall a great deal. This is why four different plasters
were assessed in the simulations: the regular exterior plaster, the thermoplaster from
CalceCanapa and two plasters from the WUFI Pro material database. As an example, in
Figure 3.15 the resulting total water content in wall layout 2.2 with two different types of
plaster is shown. The quality of the plaster is so influencing that in the case on the left,
the water content increases constantly over the years while on the right it is acceptable.
Figure 3.15: Total water content of wall layout 2.2 with two different assessed plasters,
only the plaster differs in the simulation (CalceCanapa one on the left and WUFI one on
the right)

Based on the results of the analysis, the plaster needs to be vapour permeable, or at least
have a similar vapour permeability to the adjacent materials (as seen with the Glaser
method). The plaster must both allow vapour flow from the inside to the outside and
prevent the absorption of rain to perform satisfactorily. Preferably, to deal with the issues
found in the simulation, a better performing plaster with a higher density and a lower heat
capacity, would ensure the wall to dry out faster.
Possible rain screen:
As the absorption of driving rain is the main cause of issues, the possibility of a rain screen
was assessed. This could either be a cladding system as external finishing or a bigger
roof eave. In WUFI Pro it was modelled as a ventilated facade cladding, the ventilation
is required to allow moisture flow and not create a moisture barrier. In both wall layouts
the rain screen adjusted all the assessed parameters to acceptable values within the set
limits.
The geographical location:
Both wall layouts perform better in the dryer and warmer climate of Marseilles. Also in this
case the main issues derive from rain absorption, which again comes from the south. The
outer side of the wall could on the long term be affected by this, even though the layouts
dry out faster as there is more sun radiation than in Scandinavia. Overall water contents
and RH levels are lower and closer to the set limits, for further information see Appendix
A.5.
Build-in moisture:
The built-in moisture could derive from not completely cured hempcrete before its in-
stalled. It was assessed whether the same construction built in-situ and not prefabricated
(highest possible in-built moisture) would have issues. In both cases the water contents
and the RH level would be higher in the first simulation year, but then stabilize on the
same levels of the other simulations. It is concluded that possible in-built moisture should
not cause any major damage to the wall.
Internal surface mould growth:
In none of the assessed simulation there is risk of internal surface mould growth.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
39
==PAGE:50==
Wall layout 2.2:
Figure 3.16 shows the best optimized simulation of the wall located in Lund facing south.
This is the closest possible scenario to a real one located in Copenhagen.
Figure 3.16: Best performing cross section assessed in WUFI of Wall layout 2, tempera-
tures, RH and water content throughout the section can be seen in the figure
The limits of the Glaser method made it impossible to assess wall layout 2.2 as the entire
external panel was exceeding the 100% RH. The same result was found from the WUFI
simulation: the external hempcrete panel constantly has a RH exceeding 95% and its
water content constantly exceeds 30 mass-percentage, which is definitively over the set
limits. This is because of the absorbed rain by the external plaster layer, in fact the accu-
mulated water content in between this two layers is very high. Furthermore, the external
side of the hemp fiber insulation is affected by this intense water flow. The water content
in this layer is constantly over 28%, which does not only affect the insulating properties
of the material, but could also have a long-term effect on the load-bearing bamboo struc-
ture. To conclude, it can be said that the wall can not fulfill the set requirements if the rain
absorption issue is not first solved.
40
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:51==
3.3.4
Wall layout 4
Figure 3.17 shows the results of the best south facing simulation located in Lund for what
concerns wall layout 4.
Figure 3.17: Best performing cross section assessed in WUFI of Wall layout 4, tempera-
tures, RH and water content throughout the section can be seen in the figure
This wall layout is performing considerably better as its total water content is stationary and
lower compared to wall layout 2.2. The hempcrete layer that surrounds the load-bearing
structure never exceeds a water content of 13% mass-percentage which is under the set
limit of 20%. There is a slight issue with the absorbed rain as the external side of the
hemp fibers still has high RH levels, especially in winter, and the water content fluctuates
between 22% and 33%. This is still too high to fulfill the requirements and could affect the
insulation properties of the panels. On the other hand it is way better compared to wall
layout 2.2 and it is believed that with the choice of a slightly more dense insulation panel
the issue could be solved, as less absorption would happen.
