3.4
Buildability
Throughout earlier presented process steps, several set requirements were dependent
on each other and interfered with the concept development. Also the buildability require-
ments determined if the initially chosen material concepts could be well integrated in a
simple system. To conclude whether a combined insulation and structure concept would
work as a whole, as well as create an element with most possible prefabricated parts, 3D
sketches were created for the preferred concepts.
3.4.1
3D drawings of wall element
The 3D drawings were the initial way of developing the materials into a wall element
system. The elements were combined theoretically in a visual way as if they would be
built digitally. This gave the required knowledge to evaluate the combination.
Wall element design 1: laminated bamboo structure with prefabricated insulation
panels
When creating the first wall concept, the moisture issues from wall layout 2.2 were un-
known, and the concept showed potential in a mix with structural concept 2 (Section 3.2.1).
This combination will, from now on, be called element design 1. Step 4 of the design pro-
cess (see Figure 2.4) was determined by the criteria of system integration and simple
system in the design of the prefabricated wall. From these criteria, the laminated bam-
boo beams were combined with the wall layout, by placing the hemp fibers insulation
between the structure and adding panels of hempcrete on the interior and exterior side of
the structure.
Figure 3.18: Element design 1: sandwich element with fibers and hempcrete panels
Prefabrication method
Not all manufacturing steps were possible to be prefabricated for this concept design, due
to the joints between each element and with the slabs. Therefore, for one wall element
with the dimensions of 1200 mm x 3000 mm the structure is prefabricated with only the
exterior panels attached to it. The exterior panels are attached to the structure with L-
brackets and screws. For the interior panels the L-brackets were discarded, for it was
42
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:53==
estimated that the panels should be attached with several L-brackets as the strength of
hempcrete is very low. This could create issues for the structure’s load bearing capacity
due to the holes from the screws. No pull out tests for hempcrete was performed due to
the limits of the project.
Figure 3.19: Element design 1: displaced elements
Benefits
• Interior and exterior hempcrete panels can be mass produced
• Panels are designed with tongue and groove
• Panels are attached to each other with mortar in the tongue and groove joints, for
airthightness.
• Hemp fibers can easily be placed in between the structure
• The structure will not be subjected to moisture due to the the curing process of the
hempcrete
• The panels can be created to extend above the wall, creating an insulation layer on
the exterior of the slabs
• No bolts in the interior panels
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
43
==PAGE:54==
• Streamlined workflow for the panels, as the sliding tracks can be attached to the
slabs prior to the placement of the wall elements
Issues
• Attachment of panels to structure
• Panels are hard to place in corners
• Panels above openings can be difficult to fasten
• A large part of the element must be assembled on the building site
• Thermal bridges due to metal tracks for panels
Adding to the complicated installation process on site, another issue of the concept is how
the panels are attached and carried above openings. Here they can not be supported by
the slab, and an additional bracket system needs to be implemented on the structural
beam above the opening. The panels were designed with a thickness of 140 mm, which
means that a panel above an opening with a width of 1200 mm, weighs around 35 kg.
This is an additional weight that the beam needs to carry, which limits the freedom of
the opening sizes. Additionally, the brackets could make the interior finishing with plaster
complicated and ugly.
As said, the panels were designed to be attached to the structure. On the interior side of
the elements, the metal tracks were designed, to be able to slide the hempcrete panels in
front of the structure, as well as attach them to the slabs. However, they initiate a thermal
bridge, as the tracks are placed next to the structure and no insulation material covers the
connection. The process for minimizing the thermal bridges from the metal tracks can be
seen in Figure 3.20 & 3.21.
Element design 1.1 and 1.2: attachment of the internal hempcrete panels
Figure 3.20: Element design 1.1
Element design 1.1 was an alteration created with a mini version of the sliding tracks seen
in Figure 3.19, which saved material as well as limited the thermal bridges. Furthermore,
it can simplify the assembling of the building corners, as the small bracket system gives
more freedom of moving the panels. Though for this solution, the panels must be man-
ufactured with a small indentation for the brackets to fit under the them. The brackets
44
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:55==
can be attached to the slabs with bolts prior to the placement of the elements, in order to
streamline the work on the building site.
Figure 3.21: Element design 1.2
Element design 1.2 was developed to simplify the installation process of element design
1.1. further, by attaching the hemp-panels to the slabs with L-brackets and screws. This is
a simpler version as it does not include alterations to the panels in order to fit the brackets.
This solution would save steel compared to the element design 1.1, and would make the
attachment of interior panels in corners easier.
In general the whole idea of using steel brackets was a way to simplify the attachment
of the hemp panels, but in fact, it ended up adding more and more issues. At the end a
somewhat simple version was developed, Element design 1.2, but the actual work to put
all the brackets on site would in the end create a very complicated construction process.
It is assumed that steel contributes to a large part in the environmental impact of the
element. Therefore, the amount of steel elements should be limited, and that is not the
case of this solution. The complicated construction process is mainly a result of the very
low amount of prefabrication, which is meant to simplify the process. This element design
was discarded based on the complicated process as well as the moisture issues found in
3.3.3.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo

==PAGE:56==
Wall element design 2: Bamboo stems structure as sandwich element with fibers
and hempcrete panels
Figure 3.22: Element design 2: Bamboo stems structure as sandwich element with fibers
and hempcrete panels
Before deciding which structural concept was the optimal one for the final element, the
structural analysis showed that the bamboo stem structure was the one consisting of
least material. Furthermore, the bamboo stems appears very sustainable. The stems
can be taken directly from the forest and after treatment and drying, they are ready for
use. Though as mentioned in Section 3.2.1, the geometry of the stems makes the pre-
fabrication of the structure difficult. The prefabrication method for this method is similar
to the method described in Section 3.4.1, only with a different structure type (see Figure
3.19).
For this concept the following benefits and issues where taken into account:
Benefits
• No adhesive is used to produce the stems
• The structure consists of natural materials only
46
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:57==
Issues
• The geometry of the stems makes connections difficult in all aspects
• Small air gaps due to the difficulties of wrappings insulation around the stems, pos-
sible issues with airtightness
• It is difficult to certificate the low tech joints where the stems are tied together
• Screws and bolts might splinter and lower the strength of the stem
Figure 3.23: Element design 2: displaced element
The main issues which arise from this solution, are how to connect the different element’s
components and the building elements between them. The beams must be connected
with the columns, the insulation panels to the structure and at last the elements to each
other and to the slabs. The element design was discarded based on this complicated
manufacturing and installment process, as well as the possible airtighness issues due to
the geometry of the bamboo stems.

Prefabrication method
This element was created to simplify and remove some issues from the element de-

hempcrete is cast around the structure. Where the interior and exterior hempcrete in Fig-

amount of steps on the building site is lowered.
Benefits

• The hempcrete can be cast to extend above the wall, creating an insulation layer on

• The external hemp insulation is placed across element connections to lower thermal

Figure 3.25: Element design 3: displaced elements
Despite the smaller issues for this element design, this design was chosen for all further
investigations as it has the best performing qualities both structurally and hygrothermally
(see 3.2 and 3.3.4), as also considering the buildability of the element. Only this element
design will be evaluated in the remaining part of the thesis.
To ensure that all options were considered, this wall element design was also assessed
with bamboo stems as structure. In this case, no issues with airtightness or with the
attachment of the insulation to the structure would arise. The remaining issues of attach-
ing the beams to the columns, as well as creating a standardized connection between
elements remain, and a larger investigations of possible new connections must be per-
formed. Due to the limited tests which could be performed during the thesis, it was not
possible to start these evaluations. As the laminated bamboo solution involved already
existing solutions for these connections, the concept with the laminated bamboo elements
became the main focus of the thesis.

3.4.2
Prototyping in concrete lab
After creating the 3D drawings of the elements in SketchUp various questions and as-
sumptions were left unanswered. Therefore several prototypes were created, as stated
in 2.4.5. The prototypes were used in order to test whether the assumptions made can
be justified.
From the prototypes the following observations were made:
• The small layer of hempcrete on the inside of the structure is stable.
• Corners are very brittle when not fully cured, but are hard and stable when well cast
and fully cured.
• Elements can be transported without being damaged. Only individual shivs fell of
the prototypes, as they were transported.
• The holes in the hempcrete for the bolts connections must be larger than originally
thought or designed with a slope, to allow the use of an electric tool for the bolt.
• It is possible to screw directly into the hempcrete.
• Insulation panels were reattached to the hempcrete surface three times in the same
hole, and they were still fastened after the last time.
• The structure was held in place by the hempcrete after curing.
• No bigger deterioration signs due to moisture were seen on the bamboo structure
• The designed connection between bamboo structure of different elements seems
working well.
• The surface of the hempcrete is very hard as well as flat when cured.
• The surface which was facing down on the wood plate during curing was brittle and
soft, moisture got soaked up by the support wood, which affected the curing process.
• Plastering to finish the element works well and looks nice.
Figure 3.26 shows the resulting built prototype (the element corner) of the final design.
Views from all sides as well as the detail of the developed connection system can be seen.

3.4.3
System integration
The successful integration between the insulation and structural materials was finally as-
sessed by creating the prototypes in the concrete lab. For example, it was found that the
bamboo beams and columns absorbed some of the moisture from the curing hempcrete
mixture, without creating major issues. On the other side, some practical information were
discovered, for example that the two materials can easily detach if not manufactured prop-
erly. This creates further questions, whether the possible small air-pockets between the
two materials, would create condensation issues. Only further test on the prototypes could
assess this.
However most issues were solved in an iterative process, performing the evaluations in
parallel and then finally assessing them in the prototyping phase. When one solution for
the element was chosen to solve a problem but another issue would appear, the team
would take the process one step backwards, to find a simpler solution avoiding having
too many problems or finding quick fixes. For the structure, three solutions where tested,
the ”LEGO” blocks, bamboo stem structure and the laminated bamboo structure. Where
all three were analysed and compared by strength vs. material use and stability. These
properties showed that the ”LEGO” block structure was not feasible, even though the con-
cept showed great promise and simplicity. The bamboo stem structure was initially the
solution which gave the best results based on strength versus material use, but the solu-
tions for the build-ability became too complicated due to the geometry. This finally led to
the use of the laminated bamboo structure, which in the end was the simplest and most ef-
ficient solution. The same process was performed for the insulation. The best performing
layouts on a thermal insulation level, were the walls were both hempcrete and hemp fiber
insulation were considered (wall layout 2.2 and wall layout 4). On the moisture handling
side the best performing wall was definitively wall layout 4. Wall layout 2.2 instead resulted
to have some issues with to high water content on the external side of the wall. During
the buildability assessment the initial idea was to use hempcrete panels, which were pre-
fabricated and attached on site. This solution seemed simple, but made the process on
site complicated due to the many elements to consider during construction. Furthermore,
issues with attaching elements above openings and in corners became visible as the 3D
drawings of the elements were created. So with the risk of the structure being subjected
to too much moisture during the curing process of the hempcrete, it was decided to cast
the structure inside the hempcrete. This solution gave more possible issues, but issues
which could be tested in the lab, as the team created the prototype.
Finally, the best structural concept (the laminated beams) was combined with the best
performing one on the hgygrothermal level (wall layout 4). This was made in the most
efficient way determined during the buildability assessment. The resulting final prefab-
ricated element and whether this fulfills all the set requirements will be presented in the
next chapter.
52
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:63==
4
Results
Figure 4.1: Overview sketches of the final designed prefabricated element, main function
and layers are shown
4.1
Final design
As presented in Chapter 3, the best performing structural and insulating concepts were the
laminated bamboo beams (structural concept 2) and the hempcrete wall with the external
applied insulation system (wall layout 4). This applies both for their technical properties
such as the load-bearing capacity and the thermal insulation values, as for their combina-
tion in a whole integrated building system. Furthermore, the manufacturing of the element
and its installation on-site were assessed in the buildability, Section 3.4. In Figure 4.2 an
overview of the final element is given. This chapter describes the designed structure el-
ements, the required insulation thicknesses and the optimizations made to the elements
in the evaluation process.
The assessment whether the elements would fulfil all the initially set requirements, re-
sulted in an iterative improvement process for the design. The evaluations and results
can be found in the following chapter and the detailed drawing of the final element in
Appendix A.6.

4.2.2
Stable construction
The construction is stabilized against the wind load by a bracing system, and is designed
for a building with a maximum span between stabilizing walls of 5.6 meters, which is the
set length of the slabs. The bracing system consists of one wind truss in each direction,
which can be placed together in one element or separately. One bracing system must
be used for each facade side and on all floors in the building, as the wind load affects all
sides of a building. The trusses are connected to the structure by screws, and all trusses
are made of stainless steel. The stainless steel is chosen based on the requirements of
DS/EN 10088-4:2011 [45] as the truss will be exposed to moisture in the wall element and
corrosion must be avoided.
Figure 4.3: Element with bracing system
Figure 4.4: Connection of truss to structure

failure. The additional corner pieces must be a minimum of 35 mm x 50 mm, but is only
necessary when placing a truss in the element.
For further information about the trusses see Validation of trusses in Appendix A.2.

The element resulting from the optimization has an improved U value compared to the
one calculated in Section 3.3.1, this because the hempcrete layer is 30 mm thicker (fire
safety evaluations) and the plaster is slightly thinner. The uni-dimensional calculation can
be found in Appendix A.4.
The resulting U-value not only fulfills the requirement set by the BR18 of 0.30 W/m2K,
but also reaches the levels recommended for the low energy class 2020 of 0.12 W/m2K.
Furthermore, there are no thermal bridges in the elements nor in the considered joints
(element to element, element to slab, to roof and to basement wall), to assess this, see
detailed drawing in Appendix A.6.
4.2.5
Limited moisture content
In Chapter 3.3, it was assessed that wall layout 4 was the best performing option. In the
Glaser method calculation it was the only cross section that never reached 100% of rel-
ative humidity, this means that there are no issues with moisture transport due to vapour
diffusion and deriving interstitial condensation. Furthermore, the WUFI Pro simulations
helped to overcome the limits of the Glaser method in case permeable materials are con-
sidered. The simulation interpretation resulted in several design optimizations, which can
be summarized by the following considerations:
• The final design materials were chosen considering that the vapour diffusion factor
of adjacent materials need to be of the same order of magnitude. This is so that the
moisture flow is not blocked in between materials and flows smoothly throughout
the section.
• In cold wet climates the material could absorb too big quantities of driving rain, this
issue can be solved in two ways:
– To choose an appropriate external plaster, which is vapour permeable but does
not absorb rain, or has a fast drying out capacity (bulk density and specific heat
capacity).
58
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:69==
– To install a rain screen, which could either be a roof with protruding eaves or a
ventilated facade cladding system. The ventilation would be required to avoid
additional moisture issues for the accumulation of moisture under the cladding.
A last simulation with the optimized final cross section of the wall was made to assess
whether new issues would pop up. The wall performed in a similar way to the one as-
sessed in Chapter 3.3.3 (see figure 4.8). Following are the summarized results:
• The moisture content in the hempcrete layer is within the set limits of 20 mass-
percentage as it never exceeds 13%, this means that there is no mould or fungi
issues in this layer and that the bamboo structure is well protected.
• The main issue of the wall layout is the driving rain absorption. This is why the
water content in the hemp fiber insulation is not always under the set limits (20-
25%). This could on the long term affect its insulation properties and deteriorate the
organic material. The issue is solved if the above mentioned solution for the rain
absorption are implemented. Furthermore an insulation panels with a slightly higher
density would ensure a lower water content and moisture would affect the material
less.
• There are no issues with internal surface mould growth, in-built moisture and total
water content of the wall.
• Detachment of the material and frost damage does not occur if rain absorption is
avoided.
• The wall performs better in warmer and dryer climates.
Full results of the final simulation can be found in Appendix A.5.
Figure 4.8: Cross section assessed in WUFI Pro of the resulting wall element design,
where temperatures, RH, water content and their variation throughout the section is illus-
trated.
4.2.6
Airtight
When the elements are installed on site, they must create an airtight building envelope in
integration with the other elements and building components. This was one of the main
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
59
==PAGE:70==
aspects to keep in mind during the buildability assessment. The solution for this is to use a
mortar layer between the hempcrete in the building parts, when the wall is installed on the
building site (see figure 4.9). This solution is very similar to how the regular prefabricated
concrete elements are installed and should therefore be functional and easy to implement.
The solution is also used by a hemp-lime panel company called Studio Green [46]. Finally,
it is important that as little as possible mortar goes in between the bamboo beams and the
connection as it could affect the quality of the connection and deteriorate the bamboo.
Figure 4.9: Cross sections of connection details, on the left with general floor slab, on the
right between elements.

The wall element must comply with the demands for the classification REI 60, which is
normally proven by fire tests. From the fire tests, the element is checked based on it
60
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:71==
ability to keep flames and gasses out (E) and its ability to insulate from the heat from a
fire (I). From these results, the resistance (R) of the element is determined. The notation
REI 60 means that the element must withstand the fire for 60 minutes based on the de-
mands above. Since this thesis does not involve fire tests, the wall element was run in the
software ConFire.exe. This software was used to show the maximum temperatures in the
critical points of the construction. For the insulation demand (I) the maximum temperature
rise must be below 180 degrees C°and the average temperature rise below 140 degrees
C°.
For the element, the following factors where used. The opening factor is set to 0.02 and
the fire load to 200 MJ/m2 for residence based on information from [47].
Figure 4.10: Calculation point for temperature rise from fire
The structure in the wall element must not be weakened within the first 60 minutes of a fire.
As the bamboo structure is combustible, the interior layer of hempcrete and plaster acts
as insulation for the structure. The minimum thickness for the insulation was determined
using the ConFire.exe, with the requirement, that the temperature should be below 200
degrees C°at the surface of the structure after 60 minutes [47]. The surface of the structure
is notated as point (x,y) (Figure 4.10). Using the ConFire.exe with the original 50 mm,
the temperature at the surface of the structure was calculated to exceed 200 degrees
C°. To ensure that the temperature does not exceed the 200 degrees C°at t = 60 min,
the minimum thickness for the interior hempcrete and plaster layer is 70 mm (Appendix
A.11). Therefore another 30 mm hempcrete is added to the interior layer, to benefit the
fire resistance as well as lowering the brittleness of the thin interior layer.
4.2.8
Certification of materials
Due to the laws in Europe all building materials must be certified, to ensure that they meet
the required standards. No certifications are applied to the bamboo structure during this
study, but it is a must if the wall element is to be used in a building. In Denmark FSC
certification is used to ensure that all wood used in buildings is sustainable, where a sim-
ilar certification can be applied to the laminated bamboo. Furthermore, CE-branding is
a requirement for building materials in all of Europe [48], and therefore all hemp mate-
rials used in the element are assumed certified as they are produced in Italy. For now
the bamboo beams and columns, which where used for the prototype, are produced in
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
61
==PAGE:72==
China, which has its own requirements. To allow the materials to be imported and used
in Denmark or EU in general, they must be controlled continually to ensure an identical
process, which is up to the standards of the CE-branding.
4.2.9
Acoustics
In the building regulation it is stated that acoustic class C from the Danish standard DS
490 needs to be achieved. This gives limits for the airborne sound insulation (≥55dB), the
impact sound level (≤53dB) and the noise levels (≤53dB) [49]. As this project focuses
only on the external wall element, without a definite dwelling layout, it is not possible
to define whether the acoustics insulation levels are fulfilled or not. Sound propagates
throughout the entire building components. Normally test and measurements must be
performed to empirically assess the noise levels or complicated calculation must be made
as many different parameters influence the acoustics [50]. This will not be a focus of this
report, and the acoustic design of the design will be left out for further investigations. The
only data which was given by Calcecanapa, is that the hempcrete has a sound reduction
index of 52 dB (see data sheet in Appendix A.10).
4.2.10
Light and view
In the building regulation it is stated that the window glass area in the wall must make up
min 10% of the walk-able floor area. A hypothetical window design was made for the final
prefabricated wall elements, to assess its possible dimensions and how the installation
would work (see Figure 4.11). The resulting window has a 120 cm tall and 96 cm wide
glass pane which means a glass surface of 1.15 m2. One prefabricated wall element can
cover the requirements of window surface of 11.5 m2 net floor area, which is a large span
considering that the wall element is only 2.4 m wide.
It was not possible to make any other daylight assessment, as only the wall is considered
and not an entire building. But when the window was designed in the detail drawings the
following general guidelines were kept in mind to ensure a good daylight scenario:
• The bottom part of the window is placed at a height from the floor of 85 cm to allow
the inhabitants to have a good view out.
• The window is placed as far out as possible as this allows more daylight in (due to
reflection and avoided shadowed corners). This is why the board system to attach
the window to the bamboo beams is implemented (see Figure 4.11).
• A high placement of the window in the wall allows a deeper penetration of light in
the room [51]. Therefore if the daylight scenario is not sufficient, the first thing to
change would be the window height. In this design it would not be an issue as only
the upper beam needs to be moved.
62
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:73==
Figure 4.11: Cross section of possible window installation
4.3
Aestetics requirements
4.3.1
Uniform and plane surfaces
The hempcrete elements can, if not properly flattened during prefabrication, have an un-
even surface. But even though the plastering process becomes a bit more time-consuming
by the uneven surface, an experienced worker can easily level it out during plastering.
This demand mainly applies for the internal surface, as the exterior surfaces of buildings
in urban areas of Denmark often have bricks or other decorations of the facades. For
now the exterior facade of the element is considered plastered, but as this might clash
with the preferred architectural expression of Denmark, another vapor permeable exterior
cladding can be developed.
4.4
Buildability requirements
4.4.1
System integration
The final element consists of four materials: lime plaster, hempcrete, hemp fiber insulation
and laminated bamboo beams. It is important that the different materials, its properties
and its function are integrated between each other. The system integration took part in
the choice of the material design, as the focus was to create a simply prefabrication and
installation process.
To ensure a good system integration, prototyping, 2D and 3D drawings for both the prefab-
rication and installation process of the element were performed and are shown in Section
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
63
==PAGE:74==
4.4.4 & 4.4.5. From the drawings the following issues were resolved:
• The external hemp fiber panels extends across connections of the elements, to
avoid thermal bridges.
• The hempcrete layer extends above the element to insulate the slab above.
• The structure is placed on the warm side of the element to avoid thermal losses and
its deterioration by cold climate and rain from outside.
• The hempcrete is cast around and inside the structure instead of attaching insulation
panels to it, to simplify the process.
• The hemp fiber panels replace a thicker and heavier hempcrete element, to avoid
large momentum affecting the structure, while improving the thermal insulation ca-
pacity.
From the prototyping of the element the following issues were resolved:
• The connection holes must be filled with solid material, to avoid that the filling needs
to cure or dry for a longer period.
• The connection holes must be 15 cm x 30 cm for easy access to place and fasten
the bolts.
• The surface of the element does not have to be completely plane, as the plastering
process can make the surface plane. A plane surface does make the plastering
process easier.
• The structure is attached to the hempcrete, when the hempcrete is completely cured.
The element should be moved with caution in the first days of curing, as the structure
is not attached when the hempcrete is still wet.
• Mortar is used for the connections between hempcrete surfaces
• The surface of the element must be damp before plastering, as the dry hempcrete
otherwise drags the moisture from the plaster.
• The hempcrete must cure on material which does not drag moisture, as it creates a
soft surface of the hempcrete.
• The panel must cure with the smallest surface covered, as the covering makes the
surface less hard.
• The structure is subjected to moisture during the curing process of the hempcrete,
but does not show signs of moisture or fungi issues,
64
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:75==
Figure 4.12: Section showing the layers of the final element design
4.4.2
Possibility of openings
The structural system created as a stud wall, makes it simple to have openings in the wall.
The necessary dimensions for the beams and columns around openings are designed
for the elements on ground floor in a five storey building. The sizes of the load bearing
elements above and around the opening were altered, as they obtain load from a larger
span. The beams above openings span 1.2 meters instead of the 0.6 meters, which it
was designed for. Therefore the height of the beam’s cross-section is increased to 110
mm from the original 60 mm to avoid an unacceptable deflection. The opening also gives
additional load to the columns, which causes the columns’ required width to increase from
the original 50 mm, to 70 mm. The new dimensions for the elements only apply to the
beams and columns which distribute the loads around openings.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
65
==PAGE:76==
Figure 4.13: Opening in wall element
For further information about the design values for elements around openings see Vali-
dation of laminated bamboo beams above openings and Validation of laminated bamboo
columns around openings in Appendix A.2
4.4.3
Integration of fittings/installations
Electrical installations and fittings can be installed in the elements, though the openings
and ducts for the installations must be created during the cast of the element. Ducts for
electrical cables must be attached to the structure, and cords can be lead through them.
The connection holes are utilized for placing the power outlets, to avoid creating more
holes in the element [52].
4.4.4
Simple prefabrication
As well as for the required simple installation on site, a simple prefabrication will save
time and material loss in the process. A simple prefabrication method must be standard-
ized, by developing standard sizes and solutions for openings, electrical installations, etc.
Furthermore, the fewer components there is in the facade the simpler the manufacturing
process is. The following presented steps can ensure that the prefabrication is performed
with the least issues and mistakes. Also changing the prefabrication method from pro-
duced by hand to an automated process could simplify the process.
Before the prefabrication can begin, the following elements must be ready for the process
to run smoothly:
• A mixture of 2.25 m3 hempcrete must be prepared per element, preferable with
additional 10% in case of waste during the process.
• Form-work must be oiled and ready to use with the internal dimensions of 2.4 m x
3.18 m.
• A laminated bamboo structure of five columns and two beams with the outer dimen-
sions of 2.4 m x 3 m must be constructed.
66
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:77==
• Form-work for creating the connection holes in the element.
• A plate with the dimensions 2.4 m x 3.18 m with handles on one side for planing the
hempcrete surface after cast.
• A plate with the dimensions 1.7 m x 2.46 m with handles on one side for planing the
hempcrete surface on the inner side of the connection holes.
• Plates with handles which match the areas of the hempcrete on the outer side of
the form-work, to plane the hempcrete surface where the connection holes are.

Simple system
To be competitive on the market, each element must be as easy to install as the elements
it is replacing. To ensure this, the elements must be ready for installation and are stan-
dardized with the lowest possible amount of steps to be performed on the building site. It
is particularly important to reduce the number of man-hours, as in Denmark labor is a big
part of the costs of a construction project.
For the on site process to work, the following elements must be ready at hand:
• Foundations for the building on site

• Plaster for the final finish of the elements

4.4.6
Transportable
One of the set requirements was that the elements would not be harmed during transport.
As no 1:1 scale element was created, the prototypes were used for the test of distress
during transport. After creating the prototypes in the lab, they had to be moved several
times. During the process both transport by trolley and by car was performed. This re-
sulted in single hemp shives falling from the elements, but no major parts or corners were
damaged. Based on the small tests it is concluded that the elements are fit for transport,
but further investigations on a full scale element are recommended.
4.4.7
Protected against rain
The element must not be subjected to rain during construction, especially the exterior fiber
panels. There is a large risk that the element absorbs to much rainwater, which can not
be released again. (see Section 3.3.3). To ensure that the materials are protected against
rain, all materials must be covered with plastic during transport and when being stored at
the building site. Furthermore, the materials must never be placed directly on the ground,
and they should be elevated at all times.
During construction the prefabricated elements are difficult to keep covered, therefore it is
not recommended to crane and install the elements when it is raining. When the elements
are installed on site, they must be covered until the slabs are placed on top of them.
This makes the process more difficult, but is highly recommended as all the materials in
the prefabricated element are organic. When the whole building is constructed with the
elements, the fiber panels can be attached to the exterior side of the facade. It is very
critical that the fiber panels are well covered as the low density of the material makes them
highly exposed to rain.
Looking at other building sites, mineral wool and similar materials are not covered during
construction, even though the materials are also very exposed. Therefore, it is recom-
mended to do tests of the construction process to determine the necessity to cover the
panels. Based on experience from past construction sites, there is a high need to protect
all materials during construction, as many buildings experience issues with mold after the
building is finished [53]. Furthermore, there have been several attempts to reduce the
general risk of building materials being exposed to rain during the construction process.
For instance SiteCovers [54] has developed a recyclable tent for a construction site, which
could be explored as an option to avoid moisture issues.
4.4.8
Low maintenance
The wear of the element over time is very uncertain, as the materials are not commonly
used yet. Specific data on the maintenance of the material is not available, but some of
the materials can be compared with commonly known materials. See life spans for each
material in Table 4.9. If properly maintained, the wall element is expected to have a life
span of more than 100 years. Maintenance is expected to include retouch of the plaster
on both the interior and exterior side of the facade. For smaller holes in the hempcrete
mortar can be used to close them, but it is recommended to fill larger holes in the facade
with fresh hempcrete. The curing time for the hempcrete is long, but if the facade lacks
a larger piece of hempcrete it creates a thermal bridge in the element. Exterior insulation
panels can be removed and a new panels can be attached to the facade, after which the
facade is plastered again.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo

4.4.9
Reusable
Several sources claim that both bamboo and hemp can be reused and recycled, as the
hempcrete can be broken down and reused in a new hempcrete mixture [15]. The fiber
panels however will be difficult to reuse, as they would most likely break when being de-
tached, as they are glued to the surface of the hempcrete. It is plausible that the bamboo
structures can be reused, in the same way as timber structures, though it depends of the
level of decay as well as how easily the bamboo can be detached from the hempcrete. To
avoid that single materials should be detached from the element, the whole element can
be reused, if the building is being transformed or removed before the end of its lifespan.
All the natural materials of the elements can be put in landfill at the end of their life cycle,
without any harm to the ground.
4.5
Sustainability requirements
4.5.1
Natural materials
The element fulfills the requirement of consisting of natural materials, with a few excep-
tions. The exceptions include the bolts and screws which are made out of steel and plastic,
the glue in the structure and the polyester which can be found in the fiber panels.
4.5.2
Sustainability of the final design
The sustainability of the design was assessed by performing a cradle-to-gate Life Cycle
Assessment (LCA) of the final prefabricated wall element. The result indicates the total
global warming potential of the wall element when installed on site and allows to point out
the hot spots in the manufacturing process.
Functional unit:
To start the LCA process the functional unit was established. One whole finished wall
element was taken as functional unit, this includes the prefabricated wall element, the
inner plaster and the outer insulation system, as well as the bolts that are required to
install the element on site. The main dimension of the wall can be found in Figure 4.2.
From this dimensions all the required materials quantities were calculated (see Appendix
A.7).
Life cycle inventory analysis:
To fill out the life cycle inventory, first a flow diagram of the life cycle was compiled and
the system boundaries for the modelling where set (see F igure 4.30). This was helpful
to point out all the different processes the materials have to go through before ending up
in the final element.
74
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:85==
Figure 4.30: Simplified Life Cycle flow diagram of the wall system, the red line is showing
the system boundaries for the considered model.
For the full Life Cycle flow diagram see Appendix A.7
Afterwards the life cycle inventory was compiled in tables. This means every manufac-
turing and assembling process was established, including the required amounts and pro-
duction process for the materials, the required energy, water consumption, packaging,
transport and more. In this phase a lot of research was conducted to gather all the re-
quired information. The main data providers were:
• CalceCanapa is used for reference for hemp products, and Holse & Wibroe as ref-
erence for bamboo products.
• Reference LCAs for hemp shiv and fiber production ([55] and [56]), MOSO bamboo
beams production [10] and insulation panel production [57].
• Online research for electric power of the required machinery, necessary metal and
plastic production processes for screws, bolts, and trusses etc.
The full life cycle inventory tables, with all the comments, assumptions and data sources
can be found in Appendix A.7. Ones all the required information was gathered, it was
assessed whether the processes could be found in Sima Pro or if new process had to be
created.
Results and interpretation
All the input data from the tables was inserted in the Sima Pro software and the impact
calculation was made with the CLM-IA baseline methodology. When natural renewable
materials are considered, during the production phase the CO2 absorption of the growing
plant can be taken as a reducer of the global warming potential, because it has a positive
influence on the total amount of emitted kg of CO2 equivalent. This quantity of carbon
dioxide is then released back in the atmosphere in the end-of-life of the material, when it
decomposes in landfill or it is burned in the incinerator, even though in the second case it is
source of energy or heat. In this case the end-of-life phase is not considered in the LCA,
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
75
==PAGE:86==
because the uncertainty is too big. Both the resulting impacts are shown, whether the
carbon dioxide absorption is not considered during the manufacturing (see Figure 4.31)
or it is and it does lower the total impact of the element (see Figure 4.33). This because
in the first, all the negative impacts of each main process can be seen and analysed while
in the second from the beginning the total impact is cut down by the positive influence of
the organic material.
In Figure 4.31, the total global warming potential of the wall can be seen with its main
contributors, without taking into account the amount of CO2 hemp and bamboo would
absorb while growing.
Figure 4.31: Global warming potential of the wall system, without considering the CO2
uptake from the growing plants. The total global warming potential is then subdivided in
its main contributors.
Furthermore, the impact of the packaged hemp shiv, hemp fiber panels and the complete
bamboo structure are summarized in Figure 4.32. This shows the main influence of the
material transport, the production (for example the used electricity in China for the lami-
nated bamboo beams) and the side products such as screws and bolts or the packaging
in plastic and paper.
76
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:87==
Figure 4.32: Global warming potential share of the packaged hemp shives, hemp fiber
panels and the complete required bamboo structure.
Worst case scenario was considered for the LCA, not counting the carbon dioxide uptake
from the plants and with the following considerations:
• The carbonation of the lime (further CO2 absorption by the lime binder while curing)
is not considered, as it happens over a long span of time, and it was not possible
to quantify it for the considered wall system. Regardless, the carbonation should
lower the impact of the burning and slacking process in the lime production. In this
assessment only the fact that lime has around a third of impact in the production
phase compared to cement is considered [20].
• It is assumed that metal products as screws, bolts and trusses are created from new
produced steel. It should be considered that they might be made out of recycled
steel scraps at least in a certain percentage [58].
• The plastic products such as the bolts for the insulation system, the PET fiber in the
hemp fiber panels and the plastic net are assumed to be produced and imported
from China and consisting of new plastic billets. Again it is possible, especially for
the PET fibers in the insulation, that these are made out of recycled material ([59]
and [57]).
A further iteration of the LCA would implement the considerations and assess how much
the global warming impact would decrease. Furthermore, it can be seen from the results
that surprisingly against any predictions, the transport of the bamboo beams from China
has not such a big influence on the results (only 3% on the total impact). While the trans-
port of all the materials in Europe accounts for around 25% of the total global warming
potential, in the sensitivity analysis a different type of transport is assessed. Another sur-
prising result is that the consumed electricity for the laminated bamboo beams in China
accounts for 17% on the total kg of CO2 eq. This is because a big amount of electricity
is required for the beam production and the Chinese electricity mix in SimaPro is mainly
made out of non-renewable electricity sources. Finally, a 10% share goes to the pro-
duction of the hemp shiv and fibers, which is due to land use and the ammonium nitrate
based fertilizer. However, the impact from the hemp shivs and fibers completely evens
out, when including the carbon dioxide uptake of the production.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
77
==PAGE:88==
Figure 4.33, shows the result of the total global warming potential of the wall system, when
the kg of absorbed carbon dioxide are subtracted. It is considered that while growing the
absorption is of 1.8 kg of CO2 for each kg of hemp plant stem [56] and 0.7 kg of CO2 for
each kg of bamboo stem [10]. It can be concluded that the complete wall system has a
global warming potential when installed on site of 167 kg of CO2eq.
Figure 4.33: Global warming potential of the wall system when the carbon dioxide uptake
is considered.
Sensitivity analysis
The first sensitivity analysis was made on the used calculation methodology: the CML-IA
baseline was replaced by the ILCD 2011 Midpoint+ methodology. In this case the climate
change impact of the wall system is of 706 kg of CO2eq, which is approximately 10 %
less compared to the other methodology. Nevertheless the impact share for the different
elements are almost the same: 67% for the prefabricated element, 9% for the internal
plaster and 24% for the insulation system. This means that the results are consistent
and reliable, the CML-IA Baseline probably has stricter rules and weighting factors and
therefore results in slightly higher kg of CO2eq.
Two other sensitivity analysis were performed for the two most surprising results of the
LCA: the impact of the transport methodology in Europe and the amount of used elec-
tricity in the laminated bamboo beams production. This is to assess how much the two
processes would weight on the total impact. The results are summarised in the following
table:

Even though it is not very likely that the material transport in Europe would happen by train,
it is interesting to see how much difference it would make on the final impact (-19%). The
same applies to the consumed electricity in China. The hot spots in the production chain
are not always where they were expected, and the sensitivity analysis allows to spot them
and see how much they weight in the final result. Furthermore, if the sensitivity analysis
is performed on all the main impacting process it can suggest where the production chain
should be changed for a less impacting solution. In the future the product could be even
more optimized on an environmental level by investing in new techniques or change the
processes to similar less polluting ones (for example different transport ways).

Low costs

method only includes labour, material costs and rent, which excludes taxes, scaffolding

importing transport, taxes etc. for importing the materials to Denmark.

5
Discussion
In this chapter the main limitations of the applied design methods will be presented, how
these can be overcome and which future developments would benefit the project. Fur-
thermore, the element will be compared with common building methods (prefabricated
concrete/brick or wooden elements) based on costs, environmental impacts and produc-
tion capacity. This to assess what the implementation of the developed element would
mean.
5.1
Limitations and further developments
5.1.1
Structural analysis
The main limitations for the structural analysis, was that all strength values were deter-
mined based on results from one scientific report. No further tests were performed, due
to the limited time slots the thesis team was allowed in the research labs. Strength tests
of the laminated bamboo elements would have confirmed or dismissed the used strength
values.
Looking at the beams and columns in the structure, their load bearing capacity can be
further optimized in terms of utilization of the material. To do this a different shape of
cross-section could be developed, as composite beams and columns. This could save
material in the long run, but further tests of moisture issues of the element’s thin webs, as
well as different connections must be developed, for such an optimization to be feasible.
Though before optimizing the structure for the solution, a certification and standardiza-
tion of bamboo structures must be developed. As the load-bearing capacity is based on
the standardization of timber structures, the result has an uncertainty. Furthermore, all
structural calculations must go through quality control of a certified structural engineer, to
ensure the safety of the buildings being created with the wall elements.
As the laminated bamboo is not produced in Europe, CE-branding must be applied to the
elements as it is a demand for building materials in Europe. To allow the materials to be
imported and used in Denmark or EU in general, they must be controlled continually to en-
sure an identical process, which is up to the standards of the CE-branding. Furthermore,
a certification similar to the FSC certification for construction wood, is recommended as
especially public building developers have demands for using FSC certified wood [61].
5.1.2
Hygrothermal analysis
The main limitation of the moisture evaluation for the wall elements, is the quality of the
input data. As the assessed materials are not commonly used or researched, no com-
plete database with the several required input parameters for the WUFI Pro simulations
was found. The input data was collected through several sources: the material provid-
ing companies, scientific reports on hempcrete and the material database of the program
when analogies with similar materials were possible.
There are no specific regulation or suggested limits for the simulation evaluation that con-
cerns this materials, therefore general parameters for wood and organic materials were
taken into account. These as mentioned in Chapter 2.4.4 are on the safe side and there-
fore, the interpretation of the simulation results is reliable. However, it is of interest in the
future, to repeat the assessment using data based on more specific material input. To do
so, tests of the single materials must be performed to measure their different character-
istics, which are inserted in the simulation program to assess the material combination.
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
81
==PAGE:92==
Furthermore, to have a even better evaluation of the combination, it is of high interest to
perform moisture tests on the entire wall element (as it was done in [62]). To perform this,
the element is placed in controlled climates and humidity scenarios or left in a real case
situation, with the exterior side exposed to the Danish outdoor climate and the interior to a
set indoor climate. The results of these test are expected to show the actual performance
of the whole wall throughout a set time.
The main issue resulting from the moisture evaluation, is that the external hemp fiber
insulation in some periods has an above limit water content (see Section 3.3.3), which
is due to high rain absorption. The issue can be solved by applying a better performing
plaster, installing a rain screen or using a denser insulation panel. Furthermore, the actual
acceptable water content for the panels must be determined, to make it clear when the
issues arise and which of the solutions would be the most beneficial.
The input data for the U-value calculation is all provided by CalceCanapa, and the results
are similar both in the uni- as in the bi-dimensional calculation method. Therefore the
results are considered strong and reliable.
5.1.3
Buildability
The buildabiltiy of the element was assessed on a theoretical level with 3D and 2D detailed
drawings as well as with digital development for both the manufacturing and installation
process. A scaled down prototype, a corner of the element, was built to assess all the
aspects that could not be addressed digitally. The result is an optimized system on a
theoretical level. Though in practice the only way to demonstrate, that the prefabrica-
tion and installation process works, is by creating several 1:1 elements and install them
in a test building. All the considered processes, manufacturing and installation, as well
as time and cost of the solution could then be assessed in a real case scenario. In this
way parameters such as real dimensions and weight or transport of the elements, would
play a determining role in the results. When the production method is optimized, cos-
tume machinery and production system for the elements must be developed for the most
economically beneficial process.
The construction of a test building would give the tools to assess all the parameters of
the building’s performance, where a whole building design is required, such as acoustics,
daylight scenarios, energy consumption during the use phase and indoor climate.
5.1.4
Other further developments of the project
The environmental aspect of the design can be developed further, and it should be as-
sessed and researched whether there are local alternative materials which can replace
the hemp or bamboo. The replacement would cut out all the impacts due to transport
(see Section 4.5.2) and create a local circular solution. The materials in question must
have the same technical properties (load-bearing capacity, vapour permeability, insula-
tion properties) and have similar organic structure. For example, a different fiber based
material such as seaweed could replace the hemp fiber insulation or a plant with a woody
shiv such as straw could become an alternative in the composite mixture.
The previous mentioned further researches on the prefabricated wall element, are the nat-
ural development of the design process, and will lead to a market-ready product. When
the wall concept fulfills all the necessary requirements, it is of high interest to apply the
same development process to other building components such as slabs, roof elements
and internal walls. This could create a low environmentally impacting and complete build-
ing solution.
82
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:93==
5.2
Comparison with the competitors in the industry
The competitors in the danish building sector, were found to be the masonry cavity wall
and the prefabricated wooden structure wall elements. The two competitors were anal-
ysed based on LCA and costs.
5.2.1
Sigma Estimates
Sigma Estimates was used in order to determine the man-hours and costs of the de-
veloped element (see Chapter 4.5.3), and to compare these with the competitors in the
market. All data for the production and installation of the competitors was found in the
Molio database for new constructions. Figure 5.1 shows the costs per m2 for each of the
three elements.
Figure 5.1: Cost per m2 for each considered element
To understand the prices for each element, the graphs in Figure 5.2 illustrates the cost
category division for each element.
Figure 5.2: Cost share by category (labour, material and rent) for each element. Rent has
relevant share only for the concrete/brick wall, in the other cases it is less than 5%
The costs of the element pr. m2 are similar, but the hemp/bamboo element is more similar
to the concrete element in how the costs are distributed. The reason for this, is that most
of the labour costs are based on processes from the concrete element, where only the
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
83
==PAGE:94==
bamboo structure and trusses are based on labour costs from the wooden element. The
wooden wall element stands out with a larger labour cost than the competitors, which is
due to the installation of many different individual components in the wall.

Based on the data given, the designed element is not the cheapest alternative, but is
highly competitive. To ensure that the cost of the developed element is accurate, several
actual project should be performed, and prices from experience must be developed and
included in a database. This especially applies for all hemp elements, as the bamboo
elements are very similar to wood. However the estimated costs are highly reliable on a
theoretical level, as they are based on experience from other similar materials.

all repairing and maintenance of the elements are taken out of the calculations, as they
create a large uncertainty.
Figure 5.6: Cradle-to-grave for the wooden element
Looking at the production of the three elements (cradle-to-gate perspective), the most
impacting one is the concrete/brick element (895.8 kg CO2eq). The wooden element has
the lowest impact of the three, namely 310 kg CO2eq without taking the CO2 uptake of
the pine into account. This puts the hemp/bamboo element in between the two elements
with a production impact of 761 kg CO2eq.
When taking the CO2 uptake into account the wood and hemp/bamboo elements have
respectively each an impact of -996.9 kg CO2eq and 167 kg CO2eq. For the concrete
element no CO2 uptake is considered. For the production of each element, the wood
element is the preferred element. But due to lack of data for end of life phase of the
hemp/bamboo element, no final conclusion can be drawn. It could be that in a cradle-to-
grave perspective the impact of the disposal of the designed element is lower than the
one of the wooden element. Furthermore, the production potential of the bamboo is higher
than for construction wood for at least the next 20 years [10], due to the fast growth of the
bamboo. Therefore, wood scarcity due to its increasing demand for construction can be
avoided if bamboo is utilized as a substitute.
86
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:97==
5.3
Production potential
One hectare dedicated to hemp farming produces 9000 kg of hemp shiv and 2400 kg of
hemp fibers [55]. This means that, considering the required quantities of material calcu-
lated in the LCA (see Appendix A.7) and an additional 10% of waste during the production,
one hectare approximately produces 39 prefabricated wall elements of which 29 can be
covered with the insulation panels.
For what concerns the bamboo production, only 42% of bamboo stems harvested in the
forest can be used for laminated bamboo, while the rest goes into less valuable side prod-
ucts or is used for energy production. One hectare of bamboo forest produces annually
approximately 5 m3 of this first quality products [10]. This means that a hectare of land
has the potential to produce material for the structure of 35 prefabricated elements.
Figure 5.7: Production of hemp and bamboo in 1 hectare of land
If a five-storey building and a single-family house are considered, the two main construc-
tion types in Denmark (see Chapter 1.4), the following quick estimation can be done. A
standard rectangular five-storey dwelling building, where each storey is approximately 100
m2, requires 86 prefabricated elements in the considered dimensions for its construction
(see Figure 5.8). A two storey one-family house of 120 m2 requires 22 prefabricated ele-
ments. This brings to the conclusion that if the concrete/brick technique, with an impact
of 896 kg CO2eq per element in the manufacturing phase is replaced with the hemp-
bamboo element (impact of 167 kg CO2eq) in the construction of a multi-storey building
and a single-family house respectively 62 and 16 tonnes of CO2eq can be saved.
As seen in Chapter 1.3, bamboo forests can grow in areas where only few other crops
and plants can (for example eroded slopes), and it regenerates it self as it is a grass
species. The Chinese bamboo growth potential is very high and there is no scarcity risks
for the product [10]. Therefore, the European hemp production is taken as measurement
to evaluate the production and replacement potential of the designed element.
The hemp production in Europe has been constantly increasing in the last 10 years and
by 2017 around 105,000 hectares were dedicated to hemp farming [63]. Around 50% per-
cent of this is produced in France and Italy, which are the locations where the considered
hemp products in this report are from. Furthermore, a survey from 2013 says that of the
produced fibers and shiv, respectively 26% and 16% go into the construction sector [64].
This percentage as well as the dedicated hemp farming hectares are higher nowadays,
as the building sector is becoming more interested in hemp construction and overall hemp
production is increasing, but for now they will be taken as references.
Moreover, this means that still considering the 10% of material waste during the produc-
tion, the production potential in France and Italy is 329,300 prefabricated wall elements
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
87
==PAGE:98==
of which 246,500 can be covered with the fiber insulation system.
Figure 5.8: Considered dimension of standard buildings in Denmark for production po-
tential calculation
To conclude, with the overall production potential of 246,500 complete hemp-bamboo wall
systems, as they were designed, it would be possible to build 2860 multi-storey houses
or 11200 single storey buildings. This would cover the danish new building activity of
the last year for one of the considered building types (see Figure 1.6) and save 180,000
tonnes of CO2eq. This conclusion is made by assuming that all the produced hemp in
Italy and France, dedicated to the construction industry, would be shipped to Denmark.
Furthermore, also the dimensions for the assessed building types are assumed as well
as the building footprint. However the impact of the replacement is so big that even with a
larger error margin it would be definitively worth it to invest further in the design and make
it a marketable solution.
5.4
Sustainability goals
In this final section, it is assessed which Sustainable Development Goal set by the United
Nations, would be tackled with the implementation of the designed element.
Bamboo and hemp are both sustainable materials, based on the research made not only
in this thesis, but also in other research papers. The potential shown in this report is only a
small part of the materials potential, as both materials are used for many other application,
for example food, clothes, utilities and much more. The resources can be used to ease the
pressure for forest management, lower the CO2 emissions of the construction industry and
become new income sources for rural populations. The following are the UN Sustainable
Development Goals [65] which would be supported:
5.4.1
SDG 3: Good Health and Well-being
”Ensure healthy lives and promote well-being for all at all ages”
One of the main good properties of hemp and hempcrete is that it is a vapour perme-
able material. This brings along several advantages, such as a good indoor air quality,
no mould and fungi issues and regulated indoor temperatures (see Section 1.3). A good
indoor environment is fundamental for the health of the inhabitants, as it can avoid respi-
ratory and cardiovascular diseases [65].
88
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:99==
5.4.2
Goal 7: Affordable and clean energy
”Ensure access to affordable, reliable, sustainable and modern energy for all”
From the the LCA for MOSO bamboo [10], it was concluded that almost all industrial
bamboo production is CO2 negative. This is due to the credits for bio-energy production
which the bamboo can be utilized for at the end-of-life, as well as the carbon sequestration
due to land change. This positive impacts outweigh the emissions of production and
shipping. The same can be assumed for the hemp which can also be utilized for bio-
energy. Though the end-of-life of the two materials is not taken into account in this thesis,
this is a potential which should be investigated further.
5.4.3
SDG 13: Climate Action
”Take urgent action to combat climate change and its impacts”
13.1: Strengthen resilience and adaptive capacity to climate-related hazards and natural
disasters in all countries.
Especially bamboo as a construction material is utilized for housing in areas with large
earthquake risk, due to the bamboo’s flexibility which is highly adaptable for earth quakes.
Furthermore both bamboo and hemp have big carbon uptake rates while growing, bigger
than wood for example, as they grow very fast. This is seen as a good potential to tackle
climate change.
5.4.4
SDG 11: Sustainable cities and Communities
”Make cities and human settlements inclusive, safe, resilient and sustainable”
One of the main goals in the design of the prefabricated wall was that it could be imple-
mented in new constructed multi-storey buildings. This are been built all over the biggest
cities in Denmark because of the big housing demand and fast urbanization need. If the
design would be used on a large scale, all this new developing neighbourhood could claim
an environmental sustainable urbanization process.
Furthermore as bamboo and hemp are largely produced in Asia([10] and [63]), it could
be of an high interest to assess the implementation and impacts of the wall element in
Asian context. This could decrease even more the element’s global warming potential, as
the transport share of it would be cut out, as well as offering a local, fast and sustainable
development opportunity for rural or degraded urban contexts.
5.4.5
SDG15: Life on land
”Protect and restore terrestrial ecosystems and halt all biodiversity loss”
Where the two materials can be of use to reach the sub-goals: 15.3 ”By 2030, combat
desertification, restore degraded land and soil, including land affected by desertification,
drought and floods, and strive to achieve a land degradation neutral world.” and SDG 15.b,
which calls for the integration of natural resources into developing countries including
reforestation.
By utilizing the two materials and planting it systematically in degraded land for the bam-
boo and in the rest years for the hemp, the potential of optimizing land use and for storing
millions of tons of carbon becomes great [10].
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo

==PAGE:100==
Figure 5.9: From left: SDG 2, SDG 7, SDG 11, SDG 13 and SDG 15, taken from [65]

Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
==PAGE:101==
6
Conclusion
The goal of this report was to design an alternative prefabricated wall element, made out
of the natural materials hemp and bamboo, to replace more climate-impacting materials
on a large scale. The solution was expected to be competitive with other common build-
ing techniques (prefabricated concrete-brick or wood facade walls) on a technical and
economical level while being the environmentally preferred option.
In a cradle-to-gate perspective the final prefabricated wall design has a very low impact
on the environment, the absorbed CO2 while growing (594 kg) lowers the global warming
potential of the element to 167 kg CO2eq. In the comparison with the competitive build-
ing techniques, it showed that the designed hemp-bamboo element has a lower global
warming potential than the concrete-brick element (896 kg CO2eq). This in both cases,
whether the carbon uptake of the growing bio-materials is considered or not. Compared
to the wood element it was not possible to conclude the same, as both alternatives (wood
and hemp) uptake CO2 while growing. The comparison showed that in a cradle-to-gate
perspective the wooden element would perform better (-997 kg CO2eq). However, the
absorbed carbon during the production phase is released again in the end-of-life phase
with the disposal of the materials from the wooden element. The end-of-life phase was
not considered for the hemp-bamboo element due to too big uncertainties, while it cre-
ates a very big impact for the wooden element (1596 kg CO2eq). Further development
of the LCA could prove that in a cradle-to-grave perspective the hemp-bamboo element
has a lower impact in the disposal and therefore in the whole life cycle. Furthermore,
on a short term bamboo has less scarcity issues compared to wood, therefore it can be
concluded that the element is a better choice for an immediate change in the impact of
the construction sector.
The technical design was carried out by following the Eurocodes and the requirements
set by the Danish standards. For the structure of the element, the goal was to develop
a solution with the capacity to carry the load from five storeys. The high strength of the
bamboo ensured this possibility and the element in theory has the capacity to replace the
polluting concrete-brick solution which is used now. The strength of the glue laminated
bamboo is high compared to glue laminated timber, but the stiffness of the material is very
similar. Therefore, the strength of the bamboo’s is not fully utilized, as the deflection of the
material exceeds its limit at lower loads due to its low stiffness. This issue can be tackled
by the development of composite beams and columns, to optimize the utilization of the
structural elements strength capacities. A final conclusion for the capacity of the element
can only be drawn if the standards and certifications for bamboo are developed. It can be
concluded, that the results are close to realistic, as bamboo has wood like properties, but
the standards for the material is a must for future development of the structure.
The Danish building regulations requires that a building envelope has good insulating
properties, no thermal bridges and no moisture related issues. The designed final ele-
ment reaches a very good U-value (0.123 W/m2K), which is definitively competitive with
not natural insulation materials and fulfills the requirements. The moisture related prop-
erties were difficult to assess on only a theoretical level. The final wall layout was chosen
based on the solution, which theoretically does not give interstitial condensation, and
stays within the set limits for total water content. For an improved assessment of the
moisture handling properties of the wall, lab tests on the materials and the element must
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo
91
==PAGE:102==
be performed: both of the single materials to improve the input information, and on the
wall layout chosen for the wall. A real case scenario of constructing a building with the
designed wall element would benefit the assessment of moisture issues, as it would grant
the possibility of finalizing empirically the conclusions.
Besides the performance of the element, the main requirement of being buildable is very
essential for the evaluation of the element, as without it the element would not be usable.
The method of creating a 3D model and a scaled prototype of the final element ensured
a theoretical optimization of the component. All components in the element are designed
to be simple and with a standardized solutions to create a simple construction process.
In theory the element is highly buildable, but the limitations of the used method are that
some processes and issues can be overlooked. If tested on a 1:1 scale element in a
designed building, unforeseen issues could arise. To fully understand the manufacturing
process and buildability of the element, a building for the element to be used in must be
designed and build.
The central idea for the wall element was to implement it on a large scale, which would
improve consistently the environmental impact of the construction industry. For this to
be possible, the element must be economically appealing to companies. The price of
the hemp/bamboo element is not the cheapest alternative, but it is highly competitive
according to the performed comparison. However, the uncertainty of the data for the
designed element (material import from Italy and man-hours pr m2 element), requires
further investigation. To conclude the production and installation costs with certainty, the
element must be produced in full scale and installed on site.
Furthermore, based on the evaluated production potential: there is enough cultivation of
bamboo in Asia and hemp in Europe to produce elements on a large scale. The Danish
demand, for either new construction of multi-storey buildings or one-family houses can
be satisfied, if all the hemp produced for construction in Italy and France is exported to
Denmark. This scenario is not realistic, but it gives an indication that even on a bigger
scale, the chosen materials have a large potential to handle the demand.
The architectural look of the element’s finishing is plane and uniform, as it is plastered on
both sides. This contradicts with the external facade expression of multi-storey buildings
in Danish cities where brick surfaces is often preferred. This difference could make the
element less appealing and harder to sell from an architectural point of view. To give the
element more competitive advantage, further possibilities for an external cladding must
be developed.
In theory all the set design requirements (Table 2.1) were fulfilled for the element, even
though some results are limited by the methods. The element works on a theoretical level,
but would benefit from a more practical approach, where a real case scenario is at the
highest recommendation. The element must be tested for fire resistance, acoustics, mois-
ture performance, daylight scenarios and buildability, prior to concluding that the element
is sufficient for its purpose.
To answer the research question of the thesis: yes, the designed hemp-bamboo prefab-
ricated wall element has a large potential to compete with the traditional materials used
in today’s constructions, on a large scale. The road for a market-ready product is still
long, and different research procedures must be performed, but it is highly possible. By
completing the recommended further investigations, the element will in time allow a big
step in the direction of a more sustainable construction industry.
92
Prefabricated wall element with hemp and bamboo

Perquisitions for design of structure in prefabricated load bearing wall 
element 
Description of structure 
Description of the area the wall element is to be used 
The panel is calculated to be used in a building with 5 stories with either wood or concrete slabs. A building 
like this would be 16 meters tall. These buildings would either be placed in the city or in the suburbs. The 
element is assumed to be used for residential buildings. 
No slabs or foundations are calculated.  
Description of the panels 
This thesis concerns the design and evaluation of a load bearing façade panel, which can easily substitute the 
more CO2 emitting façade panels being used in the industry today.  
The panels consist of a laminated bamboo structure, which is created as a framing structure with trusses. It 
is to create an easy transition from the traditional way to construct wood buildings, to creating buildings with 
bamboo. Each element is created of 5 columns as well as two continuous beams connecting the columns. 
This creates an element of 3 x 2.4 meters.  
Each panel is cast in hempcrete, which also creates the surface of the panels. The hempcrete is not 
considered as a load bearing element, but only as load on the structure.

Reliability class 
The calculations are based on the reliability class CC3, high reliability class. The consequence of failure gives 
a high risk of loss of human life, as the building is more than 4 stories tall. The factor K.FI is to be used when 
calculating load combinations for the elements. 
Table 1 - factor for reliability class CC3

Snow load 
The snow load on roof is determined by the formula: 
S = μ୧∙Cୣ∙C୲∙S୩ 
Where  
μ୧ = the snow load shape coefficient 
A flat roof is assumed, therefore,  μ୧ = 0.8 
Cୣ = the exposure coefficient = 1.0  
As typography assumes normal, as the buildings constructed with this façade would be very similar in size 
and shape of the surrounding buildings.  
C୲ = the thermal coefficient = 1.0 
S୩ = the characteristic value of snow load on the ground = 1.0 kN/m2  
S =  0.8 ∙1.0 ∙1.0 ∙1.0 𝑘𝑁
𝑚ଶ= 0.8 𝑘𝑁
𝑚ଶ

Structural analysis 
Horizontal and vertical loads 
The vertical loads consist of self-weight, imposed loads including snow loads. These loads will be distributed 
from the slabs in the building to the beam in the wall element. The beam is simply supported by each of the 
columns, and the load from the beam is distributed equally to the supporting columns. The load is then 
distributed from the columns to the beam in the bottom of the element. Where the load is distributed from 
the element, to the slab below.  
The elements are designed with bamboo trusses, which is to stabilize the elements.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Wall element 
The wall elements consist of a traditional stud wall structure, using laminated bamboo instead of glue 
laminated wood. In each panel the columns are placed with 600 mm from center to center, with a beam 
spanning in top and bottom. The calculations are conducted for one panel in an element, with two columns, 
600 from center to center. As well as two beams, a top and bottom, which spans from column to column. 
Figure 3 - Bamboo structure with truss

prefabricated load bearing wall element
